Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Haqiqah and Majaz: The Metaphysical Foundation for Hanafi Usul
Explaining haqiqah and majaz in Allah's attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish haqiqah from majaz in the context of existence, identify the Hanafi proof for Allah's exclusive real existence, and apply the concept to understand metaphorical language in legal texts.

Core Idea
Only Allah's existence is haqiqah; all contingent existence is majaz because it depends on Him.

This passage dives into the foundational Hanafi metaphysical distinction between real (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for usul al-fiqh because it shapes how we interpret words—whether they are used in their original or transferred sense. The author uses a Quranic proof and rational argument to establish that only Allah's existence is truly real, while everything else exists only through Him. This theological underpinning ensures that when mujtahids encounter ambiguous terms like 'hand' or 'face' in the Quran, they know to interpret them as majaz (metaphorical) when applied to Allah, upholding tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Identify Key Terms
Extract the definitions of haqiqah, majaz, and yaqin as presented in the passage. Note how they are defined both linguistically and terminologically.
Pay attention to the author's distinction between essential and dependent existence.
2
Trace the Haqiqah-Majaz Distinction
Follow the argument that only Allah's existence is haqiqah because it is self-subsistent (qaim bi dhatihi). All contingent beings (mumkinat) exist only through Allah, making their existence majaz.
The rational proof: the contingent is essentially non-existent; its existence is by attribution to the Necessary Being.
3
Examine the Quranic Proof
Locate the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' and understand how it supports the claim that contingent existence is metaphorical (majaz).
The verse indicates that everything besides Allah is perishing in itself, implying its existence is not real but dependent.
4
Connect to Usul al-Fiqh
Understand how this metaphysical distinction applies to the interpretation of texts. When a word like 'hand' is used for Allah, it cannot be haqiqah (since Allah is not physical); it must be majaz. This principle guides the mujtahid in dealing with ambiguous expressions.
This is key for the Hanafi approach to sifat (attributes) and for understanding the categories of amm and khass.
5
Define Yaqin
Note the definition of yaqin as a firm belief conforming to reality with no possibility of its opposite. This is the highest rank of faith and contrasts with zann.
Yaqin is required for certain legal rulings and for the foundation of usul principles.

ذات میں نیست ہیں ایک نیست دوسرے نیست کو کیا بہت بنا سکے ہست بنانا اسی کی شان ہے جو آپ اپنی ذات سے ہست حقیقی و ہست مطلق ہے، مثلج الصدر الایمان القدر )
In the form of maf'ul, it means 'filled'. As it is said: 'The vessel was filled so it became full' i.e., it was filled so it overflowed. At that time the meaning will be: 'He filled certainty in the hearts of the believers,' i.e., He filled the hearts of the believers with certainty. It is also possible that طبع with tashdid and without tashdid, both forms, active and passive, are possible. If active, it means 'filled'; if passive, it means 'made firm'. Then the meaning of the phrase will be: 'Certainty was made firm and rooted in the hearts of the believers.' Certainty is called knowledge and removal of doubt, as in the Sihah. In terminology, certainty is a type of tasdiq (affirmation), and it is the name of a firm, rooted belief that conforms to reality and does not allow any possibility of its opposite. It is among the highest ranks of faith. His statement: 'Our Lord, to You belongs the truth, truly, and every metaphor is false.' The author said: 'In the construction, either it is a maf'ul mutlaq from a deleted verb 'ahaqqu', i.e., 'I make it right', or it is a hal from 'haqiqah', the estimation being: 'The state of the truth established for You being true, matching reality.' The meaning is: O our Lord, Your existence and proof are real, true, and not subject to decay, because Your existence is essential—You are not in need of any other in Your essence and existence. And besides You, all possible and created things do not have real existence but metaphorical, because they are in need of You for their existence. Their existence is due to Your essence and Your existence. And whatever existence depends on the existence of another, its existence is not real but metaphorical. Therefore, the existence of all contingent things is metaphorical. One logical reason for the metaphorical nature of the existence of contingent things is that the contingent, in consideration of its own essence, is an instance of non-existence. It is not that the essences of contingents necessitate non-existence, otherwise the contingent would not remain possible but would become impossible. Nor is it that non-existence is more appropriate for the essence of the contingent, because essential appropriateness is difficult, as is correct in its place. Rather, the meaning of the contingent being an instance of non-existence is that the contingent, due to its non-being, is not in need of anything; rather, the absence of the cause of existence is sufficient for its non-being. Because the effect of the absence of the cause in the effect is that the cause of existence has no effect in the effect. The gist is that in the contingent, the cause of existence is entirely absent, so in itself it is perishing and false. The Quran points to this: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' As for the existence of contingent things, it is established by being attributed to the Self-Subsistent, Real, i.e., God Almighty, or by looking at their efficient cause, the essences of contingents don the garment of existence and become existing essences. Nevertheless, applying existence to them is metaphorical because the existence of the cause is stronger, more rightful, and essential compared to the existence of the effect. Just as water heated by the sun is called 'sun water' even though the water does not retain the sun, yet it is called sun because it is heated by the sun. Such is the condition of contingents: in themselves they have no existence; their existence is due to the existence of the Real Maker. And this is metaphor. The great scholar of Bareilly discussed this point regarding the reality of the possible and necessary existence, writing: 'Whether essence or attribute, act or state, bringing a non-existent thing out of non-existence and clothing it in the garment of existence—that is His work. He did not give this to anyone's choice, nor could anyone choose it. All created things are in themselves nothing; what can a nothing create for another nothing? Making something exist is His attribute alone, Who from His own essence is Real Existence and Absolute Existence. So settled the breast of faith, the power.'
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'ربنا لک الحقیقة حقاً وکل مجاز' (Our Lord, to You belongs the truth, truly, and every metaphor is false). The author begins by explaining the word 'طبع' (tab'a) in a previous context, then defines 'یقین' (yaqin, certainty) as a firm belief conforming to reality with no possibility of opposites. The main discussion distinguishes between haqiqah (real existence) and majaz (metaphorical existence). The author argues that only Allah's existence is real and essential, while all contingent beings have metaphorical existence because they depend on Allah. A rational proof is provided: the contingent is in itself non-existent; its existence is solely by attribution to the Necessary Being. A Quranic proof is cited: 'کل شئی هالک الا وجھہ' (Everything is perishing except His Face). The passage also includes a discussion by a later scholar (Fazil Bareilly) affirming that bringing things into existence is solely Allah's prerogative, not within the power of any creature. This establishes the Hanafi theological foundation for understanding metaphorical language in legal texts.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): Real existence that is essential, self-subsistent, not dependent on another.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical existence that is dependent on a real cause, not self-subsistent.
یقین (yaqin): A firm, rooted belief conforming to reality with no possibility of its opposite; a type of tasdiq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the existence of contingent things considered metaphorical (majaz)?
A) Because they are not real at all.
B) Because their existence depends entirely on Allah, who is the only real existent.
C) Because they are created by human choice.
D) Because the Quran calls them 'perishing'.

Key Takeaway
Only Allah's existence is haqiqah; all contingent existence is majaz because it is dependent on Him, a principle that guides interpretation of ambiguous texts in usul al-fiqh.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Meanings of 'Amr' (Command) in Hanafi Usul
Definition and expansion of the term 'amr' (command) in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of 'amr' and explain why, in Hanafi usul, true command belongs to Allah alone, while human commands are metaphorical.

Core Idea
‘Amr’ means act, request for an act, or specific speech; only Allah’s command is real.

This lesson analyses the term 'amr' (command) in usul al-fiqh, as discussed by the commentator. The passage distinguishes between the real command of Allah and the metaphorical command of human authorities. Understanding this distinction is foundational to grasping how obligation (wujub) originates in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of 'amr'
List the three possible interpretations of the term 'amr' as presented in the passage: (1) an act, (2) a request for an act (the technical usul meaning), (3) a specific utterance.
Only the second meaning is the technical usul definition; the others are linguistic uses.
2
Distinguish between haqiqah and majaz in commands
Understand that 'haqiqah' (real) applies to Allah's command because He is the sovereign owner and controller of all affairs. 'Majaz' (metaphorical) applies to commands of rulers, judges, etc., because their authority is derived from Allah's permission.
This distinction is theological and affects how we understand the binding force of human commands.
3
Explain why only Allah's command is real
Allah alone possesses absolute ownership (mulk), control (tasarruf), and superiority (istila'). Therefore, His command creates real obligation. Any human command is effective only insofar as it is authorized by Allah.
The commentator cites Quran 40:16 to emphasize that on Judgment Day all false claims to authority vanish.
4
Apply this understanding to the concept of obligation
In Hanafi usul, when the Qur'an or Sunnah uses an 'amr' (imperative), the default ruling is obligation (wujub) because the command comes from the real Commander. Human commands, even of a ruler, do not create obligation in themselves unless they convey divine command.
This principle underpins the Hanafi position that the obligation of obeying a ruler is ultimately derived from obedience to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قــولـه ولك الامر تحقيقاً وكل مجاز - الامر‘ سے فعل ، یا طلب فعل یا قول مخصوص مراد ہے۔ معنی یہ ہوگا کہ سارے امور ، جملہ اوامر اور قول مخصوص سب تیرے ہی لئے ہیں اس لئے کہ تو ہرشئی کا مالک و مختار ہے اور غالب ہے۔ اور سب مملوک و مغلوب اور تیرے زیر فرمان ہیں ۔ امر سے طلب فعل یعنی امر کا معنی اصطلاحی مراد لینے پر معنی یہ ہوگا کہ علی سبیل الاستعلاء امر تیرے ہی لئے ثابت ہے اس لئے کہ علو مجد تیرے ہی لئے ہے تیرے سوا کے لئے نہیں ۔ رہے دنیاوی سلاطین و حکام، اولی الامر وملوک اور ملکوتی ذوات قدسیہ کا بعض امور کا مالک ہونایا حکم فرمانا تو یہ تیرے مالک بنانے سے اور تیری ہی اجازت و اذن سے محض تیرے فضل و کرم سے ہے ورنہ حقیقت تو یہ ہے کہ ظاہر و باطن اور ماضی و حال ہمیشہ از لا وابدا تو ہی سارے امور کا مالک تجھی سے سارے امور ثابت اور تیرے ہی لئے جملہ اوامر باقی ہیں چنانچہ اس حقیقت کا اظہار قیامت کے دن ہوگا۔ ارشادربانی ہے لمـن ملک اليوم لله الواحد القهار - والى الله ترجع الامور
His saying: 'And the command is truly Yours, and everything else is metaphorical.' By 'amr' is meant either 'act' or 'request of an act' or 'specific speech.' The meaning would be: All affairs, all commands, and specific speech are all for You, because You are the owner, controller, and dominator of everything. And everything is owned, dominated, and under Your command. Taking 'amr' in the sense of 'request of an act' – i.e., the technical meaning of command – the meaning would be: The command (with superiority) is established for You alone, because sublime majesty is for You alone, not for others. As for worldly rulers, judges, leaders, kings, and celestial holy beings having ownership of some matters or issuing commands, that is by Your making them owners and by Your permission and authorization, purely out of Your grace and generosity. Otherwise, the reality is that in the manifest and hidden, past and present, always and forever, You alone are the owner of all affairs; from You all affairs are established, and for You all commands remain. Indeed, this reality will be manifested on the Day of Judgment. Divine revelation says: 'Whose is the kingdom today? It is Allah's, the One, the Irresistible – and to Allah all affairs return.'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical commentary on the phrase 'wa laka al-amru haqiqatan wa kullun majaz.' The commentator explains the term 'amr' (command) in three possible senses: (1) an act, (2) a request for an act (the technical usul meaning), or (3) a specific utterance. In all interpretations, the true and real command belongs to Allah alone. Any authority or command exercised by worldly rulers or spiritual beings is only metaphorical, granted by Allah's permission. This reinforces the Hanafi theological principle that the ultimate obligator in legal obligations is Allah, and human commands are only binding insofar as they are derived from divine authority. The commentator also references the Qur'anic verse (40:16) to emphasize that on Judgment Day, all claims to ownership and command will be nullified, leaving only Allah's sovereignty.

Key Terms
امر (amr): Command; here discussed as having multiple meanings: act, request for act, or specific speech.
استعلاء (istila'): Superiority; the manner of commanding from a position of high authority.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical; applied to commands of human rulers because they are derivative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'amr' include according to the passage?
A) Only request for an act
B) Act, request for act, or specific speech
C) Only speech
D) Only command with superiority

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the term 'amr' has three possible meanings, but in all cases true command (haqiqah) belongs to Allah alone, while human commands are metaphorical (majaz).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Priority of Real vs Compound Definition in Usul al-Fiqh
Dispute on prioritizing simple vs compound definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of definitions (hadd haqiqi and hadd idafi) and evaluate the Hanafi position on their order in usul works.

Core Idea
The compound definition is prioritized over the real definition in Hanafi usul to avoid repetition and maintain completeness.

When introducing a new discipline, scholars often debate the order of definitions. In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a key dispute arises between those who advocate for the simple real definition (حد حقیقی) and those who prefer the compound definition (حد اضافی). This lesson unpacks the rational arguments that lead the verifying Hanafi scholars to prioritize the compound definition in the introduction of usul works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definition types
Distinguish between a simple real definition (حد حقیقی) that captures the essence without reference to other terms, and a compound definition (حد اضافی) that involves a modifier and modified (mudaf and mudaf ilayh).
The real definition is considered original and simple; the compound is derivative and composite.
2
Understand the default rule
The author initially argues that the real definition should come first because the original takes precedence over the branch, and the simple over the compound.
This is a general principle in usul: الأصل مقدّم على الفرع.
3
Analyze the verifiers' objections
The verifying scholars (محققین اصولیین) give three proofs for prioritizing the compound: (1) the real is derived from the compound; (2) defining the compound first avoids repeating 'fiqh'; (3) placing the real first either leaves the definition incomplete or turns it into a compound.
These are rational ('aqli) arguments, not textual.
4
Follow the author's resolution
The author accepts the verifiers' position and places the compound definition first in his own work, following scholars like the author of al-Tanqih.
This shows that later Hanafi scholarship favored the compound-first order.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the real definition comes first, it stays simple but risks incompleteness or repetition; if the compound comes first, it streamlines the introduction by defining 'usul al-fiqh' once clearly.
This affects how the subject is introduced to the reader.

Source Text

میں پورے علم کی ایک تعریف ہے اس کے برخلاف حد اضافی میں مضاف اور مضاف الیہ کی الگ الگ تعریف ہے غرض لقمی اصل ہے اور مفرد ہے اور اصل فرع پر اور مفرد مرکب پر مقدم ہوتا ہے لہذ العھی کو اضافی پر مقدم کیا جائے گا کہ اضافی فرع اور مرکب ہے۔
حداضافی کی تقدیم کے وجوہ :۔ خیال رہے قول مذکور کے برخلاف حضرات محققین اصولیین نے لقمی پر اضافی کو کئی وجوہ سے مقدم کیا ہے۔
(۱) حدیقی ، مرکب اضافی سے منقول ہے اور حد اضافی منقول عنہ، اور منقول عنہ ، منقول سے مقدم ہوتا ہے۔
(۲) حدیقمی میں فقہ ملحوظ و معتبر ہے لہذا پہلے ہی سے حد اضافی کو مقدم کر کے اس کی تعریف کر دینا انسب ہے تا کہ حدیقی میں بار دیگر فقہ کے ذکر کرنے کی ضرورت نہ رہے ورنہ تکرار ہوگا جیسا کہ علامہ ابن حاجب وغیرہ نے مکر رذ کر کیا ہے۔
(۳) اگر اصول فقہ صدیقی کو مقدم رکھ کر اس میں ماخوذ لفظ فقہ کی تعریف نہ کی جائے تو تعریف تام نہ ہوگی اور اگر فقہ کی تعریف کر دی جائے تو حدیقی مفرد نہ رہ جائے گا بلکہ مرکب ہو جائے گا اور اس تقدیر پر حداضافی میں مضاف الیہ فقہ کی تعریف کرنا تکرار ہوگا لہذا حد اضافی مرکب نہ ہوکر مفرد ہو جائے گا حالانکہ یہ اصولیین کی تصریح کے خلاف ہے، اس لئے مصنف نے محققین جیسے صاحب تنقیح وغیرہ کی پیروی کرتے ہوئے پہلے حد اضافی کو ذکر کیا تو کہا اماحدہ مضافاً۔
In it, there is one definition of the entire knowledge. In contrast, in the compound definition, there is a separate definition of the modifier and the modified. In short, the real definition is the origin and is simple, and the origin is preferred over the branch, and the simple over the compound. Therefore, the real definition will be given precedence over the compound one, because the compound is a branch and a compound. Reasons for giving precedence to the compound definition: Note that contrary to the mentioned statement, the verifying jurisprudential theorists have given precedence to the compound over the real for several reasons: (1) The real definition is derived from the compound definition, and the compound definition is that from which it is derived, and that from which it is derived is given precedence over what is derived. (2) In the real definition, fiqh is considered and taken into account, so it is more appropriate to first give precedence to the compound definition and define it so that in the real definition there remains no need to mention fiqh again, otherwise repetition would occur, as Allamah Ibn al-Hajib and others have done repeatedly. (3) If the real definition of usul al-fiqh is given precedence and the word fiqh taken in it is not defined, then the definition would not be complete; and if fiqh is defined, then the real definition would no longer be simple but become compound, and in that case defining fiqh in the compound definition would be repetition. Hence, the compound definition would become simple instead of compound, contrary to the specification of the jurisprudential theorists. Therefore, the author, following the verifiers like the author of al-Tanqih and others, first mentioned the compound definition and said: 'As for the introduction, it is concerning the definition of usul al-fiqh...'.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among Hanafi usul scholars regarding the order of definitions in the introduction of a work on usul al-fiqh. The author initially argues that the simple, real definition (حد حقیقی) should precede the compound definition (حد اضافی) because it is original and simple, while the compound is derivative and composite. However, the author then cites the view of the verifying scholars (محققین اصولیین) who give precedence to the compound definition for three rational reasons: (1) The real definition is derived from the compound definition, and the source is prioritized over the derivation. (2) The real definition incorporates the term 'fiqh', so defining the compound definition first avoids repeating the definition of fiqh later. (3) If the real definition is placed first, one either leaves 'fiqh' undefined (making the definition incomplete) or defines it (making the definition compound and causing repetition in the compound definition). Thus, the author ultimately follows the verifiers by presenting the compound definition first.

Key Terms
حد حقیقی (hadd haqiqi): A definition that is simple and covers the essence of the thing defined without reference to another compound term.
حد اضافی (hadd idafi): A definition that involves a modifier and the modified (mudaf and mudaf ilayh), making it compound.
محققین (muhaqqiqin): Verifying scholars—those who carefully investigate and establish the soundest positions in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers (محققین), why should the compound definition (حد اضافی) be given precedence over the real definition (حد حقیقی) in the introduction of usul al-fiqh?
A) Because the compound definition is simpler.
B) Because the real definition is derived from the compound definition, and the source takes precedence.
C) Because the real definition does not include the term fiqh.
D) Because the compound definition is the only accepted definition among Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi verifiers prioritize the compound definition over the real definition in usul introductions to avoid repetition and ensure a complete definition.

The Later Definition of Fiqh and Its Defense
Defines fiqh and refutes two logical objections using malakah and amarat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of legal rulings from detailed evidences' is defended by interpreting 'knowledge' as malakah and 'evidences' as amarat.

Core Idea
Fiqh is a faculty (malakah) in the mujtahid, not mere knowledge — so 'I don't know' does not negate it.

The later Hanafi usul scholars defined fiqh as knowledge of legal rulings from their detailed evidences. This definition faced two logical objections: it seemed not universal (since mujtahids sometimes say 'I don't know') and not exclusive (since a learned muqallid also knows rulings). In this passage, the commentators defend the definition by clarifying that 'knowledge' here means a faculty (malakah) and 'evidences' means indicators that produce zann (amarat) — which is exclusive to the mujtahid. We will walk through these objections and responses to understand how the definition remains valid.

Guided Path
1
State the definition and its critical terms
Identify the core definition of fiqh: 'ilm, ahkam, adillah tafsiliyyah. Note that this is the later (muta'akhkhirin) definition.
2
Understand the universality objection
The objection that 'la adri' (I don't know) breaks universality: if the definition requires knowledge of all rulings, then a mujtahid who says 'I don't know' would not have fiqh.
3
Understand the exclusivity objection
The objection that the definition includes a learned muqallid: if 'rulings' is taken generally, anyone who knows a ruling (even by following) would be included.
4
Apply the first response: malakah
By interpreting 'ilm as malakah (faculty), the definition means the potential to derive rulings, not actual knowledge of every ruling. Thus 'I don't know' does not negate the faculty.
Malakah is a stable quality; a mujtahid retains it even when temporarily unaware.
5
Apply the second response: amarat
By interpreting 'adillah' as amarat (indicators producing zann), the definition restricts fiqh to those who derive rulings through their own istidlal (ijtihad). The muqallid relies on the mujtahid's statement, not his own derivation, so he is excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : "وعرفوه بانه العلم بالاحكام الشرعية عن ادلتها الفصيلية واورد ان كان المراد الجميع فلا ينعكس لثبوت لا ادرى او المطلق وفلا يطرد لدخول المقلد العالم واجيب بانه لا يضر لا ادرى لان المراد الملكة فيجوز التخلف وبأن المراد بالادلة الامارات وتحصيل العلم بوجوب العمل بتوسط الظن من خواص المجتهد اجماعاً واما المقلد فمستنده قول مجتهده لا ظنه ولاظنه فاعرف الفرق حتى لاتقل مثل من قال كما أن مظنون المجتهد واجب العمل علیه کذالک على مقلده فهما سيان نعم يلزم أن يكون عن العلم بوجوب العمل بالاحكام لا العلم بها الا أن يقال انه رسم فيجوز باللازم وفيه مافيه ومن ههنا علمت اندفاع ماقيل الفقه من باب الظنون فكيف يكون علماً على أن العلم حقيقة فيما ليس بتصور ايضاً ترجمه : اور متاخرین نے فقہ کی تعریف یوں کی ہے احکام شرعیہ کو اس کے ادلہ تفصیلیہ سے جاننا۔ اس تعریف پر اعتراض کیا گیا ہے کہ فقہ کی تعریف میں مذکور احکام سے اگر جمیع احکام مراد ہیں تو تعریف جامع نہیں کیونکہ فقہاء سے لا ادری ثابت ہے یا مطلق احکام مراد ہیں تو تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں رہی کیونکہ مقلد عالم اس وقت اس میں داخل ہو رہا ہے۔ اور اعتراض کا یہ جواب دیا گیا کہ احکام سے جمیع احکام مراد ہیں پھر بھی لا ادری تعریف کے لئے مضر نہیں کیونکہ تعریف فقہ میں علم سے مراد ملکہ ہے لہذا تخلف ممکن ہے اور یہ بھی جواب دیا گیا ہے کہ ادلہ سے امارات مراد ہے (امارات ظن کو کہتے ہیں ) اور وجوب عمل کے علم کا حاصل کرنا بواسطہ ظن اجمالی طور پر مجتہد کا خاصہ ہے رہا مقلد تو اس کا مستند اس کے مجتہد کا قول ہوتا ہے اس کے حق میں مقلد کا ظن نہ ظن مجتہد معتبر ہے اس لئے فرق سمجھوتا کہ ان لوگوں کی طرح تم بھی نہ کہنے لگو کہ جس طرح مجتہد کا مظنون مجتہد کے لئے واجب العمل ہوتا ہے اسی طرح اس کے مقلد کے لئے بھی ہوتا ہے کہ وہ دونوں کے دونوں برابر ہیں۔ البتہ اس وقت لازم آئے گا کہ فقہ وجوب العمل بالاحکام کے علم سے عبارت ہے وہ علم بالاحکام کا نام نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہا جائے کہ رسم ہے لہذا الازم سے تعریف جائز ہے اور اس میں بھی وہ ہے جو ہے اور یہیں سے تمکو اس اعتراض کے اندفاع کا علم ہو گیا ہو گا جو اس مقام پر کیا جاتا ہے، کہ فقہ باب ظنون سے ہے تو اس پر علم کا اطلاق کیسے ہو گا ؟ علاوہ ازیں علم کا اطلاق در حقیقت غیر تصور پر بھی ہوتا ہے۔
The text of al-Muslim: 'And they defined it as knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences. And it was objected: if by rulings all is meant then the definition is not universal because of the existence of 'I do not know', or if absolute then it is not exclusive because it includes the muqallid and the scholar. And answered: that 'I do not know' does not harm because what is meant by knowledge is malakah, so non-actualization is possible. And that by evidences are meant signs, and acquiring knowledge of the obligation to act through the medium of assumption is a special characteristic of the mujtahid by consensus. As for the muqallid, his reliance is on the statement of his mujtahid, not his own assumption nor the mujtahid's assumption. So know the difference so that you do not say like those who say: just as the mujtahid's assumption is binding upon him, so too upon his muqallid—they are equal. Rather, it necessitates that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act, not knowledge of the rulings, unless it is said that it is a definition by concomitant (rasm), then it is permissible. And there is that. And from here you know the refutation of what is said: fiqh is from the domain of assumptions, so how can it be knowledge? Although knowledge is reality in what is not a mental conception as well.' Translation: The later scholars defined fiqh as knowing the legal rulings from their detailed evidences. Objections were raised: if by rulings all are meant, then the definition is not universal because 'la adri' (I don't know) is established from fuqaha'; if absolute rulings are meant, then the definition does not exclude non-mujtahids because the scholar muqallid would be included. The answer: 'all rulings' is meant, yet 'la adri' does not harm because knowledge in the definition means malakah (faculty), so non-actualization is possible. Also, by evidences are meant signs (indications that produce zann), and acquiring knowledge of the obligation to act through zann is specific to the mujtahid by consensus. The muqallid bases his action on the statement of his mujtahid, not his own zann. So understand the difference so that you do not say like those who claim that the mujtahid's zann is binding on both equally. Rather, it would necessitate that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act, not knowledge of the rulings themselves. But if it is said that it is a definition by concomitant (rasm), then it is permissible. From here you know the refutation of the objection: fiqh is from the realm of assumptions, so how can it be called knowledge? Additionally, 'ilm applies to non-conceptual things as well.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the later (muta'akhkhirin) definition of fiqh: 'knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences'. It then addresses two main objections. First, if the definition intends all rulings, then it fails to be universal because scholars sometimes say 'la adri' (I don't know). The response: 'knowledge' here means a malakah (a faculty or aptitude), not actual knowledge; thus a mujtahid can possess the malakah even if he does not know a particular ruling at a given moment. Second, if 'rulings' is taken in an absolute sense, then the definition would include a learned muqallid (someone who knows rulings but merely follows a mujtahid). The response: 'evidences' here means 'amarat' (indicators that produce zann, not certainty), and acquiring knowledge of the obligation to act through zann is exclusive to the mujtahid. The muqallid's authority is the word of his mujtahid, not his own zann. Thus the definition is sound. Additionally, the passage distinguishes between knowing the rulings directly and knowing the obligation to act on them; fiqh can be defined as the latter (a definition by necessary concomitant). Finally, it refutes the claim that fiqh cannot be 'ilm (knowledge) because it is based on zann: 'ilm can apply to non-conceptual realities as well.

Key Terms
الملكة (al-malakah): A faculty or aptitude within the scholar, even if not actualized at all times
الامارات (al-imarat): Signs or indicators that produce zann (predominant assumption), not certainty
العلم (al-ilm): Knowledge, but in this definition it means malakah (faculty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of legal rulings from their detailed evidences' not include a learned muqallid?
A) The muqallid does not have 'ilm.
B) The muqallid does not derive rulings from detailed evidences.
C) The muqallid's knowledge is based on zann not yaqin.
D) The muqallid is not a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is a faculty (malakah) possessed by the mujtahid, enabling him to derive rulings from indicators (amarat) that produce zann; a muqallid does not have this faculty.

The Impossibility of Real Definitions for Codified Sciences
Setting up the deniers' objection by distinguishing external and mental parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between external and mental parts, and apply the deniers' objection to show why a codified science cannot be defined by a real definition (حدی).

Core Idea
A science's issues are external parts, not mental; thus its definition can only be descriptive, not real.

This passage presents a foundational objection from the deniers of real definitions. They argue that the constituent parts of a codified science—its specific issues—are not predicable on the science itself, making them external parts. Therefore, any definition in the preface must be descriptive, not real. To understand this, we first clarify the two types of parts: external and mental.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish external and mental parts
Define and contrast external parts (non-predicable, not united with essence) with mental parts (predicable, united with essence). Use the examples of a house and a human.
External parts are like walls and roof; mental parts are like genus and species.
2
Classify the parts of a science
Identify that a science's specific issues (or their perceptions) are not predicable on the science nor on each other, so they are external parts.
The text states: 'it is not correct to predicate any one issue on that science, nor one issue on another.'
3
Apply the definitional consequence
Since a real definition requires mental parts (genus and species), the definition of a science cannot be real; it must be descriptive (رسمي).
The author concludes that any preface definition can only be descriptive.
4
State the two reasons for impossibility
Understand the two objections: (a) the definition would be both external (preface) and internal (science), a contradiction; (b) it would cause circularity (دور) and self-dependence (توقف الشئ على نفسه).
The reasons are presented after 'There are two reasons why it cannot be a real definition.'

تمهید: دو باتوں پر مشتمل ہے ایک یہ کہ مقدمہ جس پر علم وفن کا شروع کرنا موقوف ہو جس میں فن کی تعریف، موضوع اور غرض وغایت کا بیان ہوتا ہے وہ مقدمہ فن سے خارج ہوتا ہے کما ھوا تحقق فی موضعہ ۔ ورنہ تو تف الشئی علی نفسہ کا اعتراض وارد ہو گا۔

دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ شئی کی اجزاء حقیقیہ وہ ہوتی ہیں جس کی طرف شئی اپنے قیام میں محتاج ہو یا جوشئی کے قوام کی علت بنے وہ علت اس شئی کی اجزاء حقیقیہ کہلاتی ہے ۔ پھر اجزاء حقیقیہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں اجزاء خارجیہ، اجزاء ذ ہنیہ ۔

(۱) اجزاء خارجیہ شئی کی اجزاء خارجیہ وہ کہلاتی ہیں جو اپنے جعل ووجود میں ذات کے ساتھ متحد نہ ہو نہ ہی ذات پر محمول ہو اور نہ اجزاء خارجیہ میں سے کوئی جز ایک دوسرے پر محمول ہوتا ہے۔ جیسے کہ بیت کے لئے جدا رو تلف اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں جو بیت پر محمول نہیں ہوتے نہ ہی جدار وسقف میں سے کسی کا دوسرے پر حمل صحیح ہے چنانچہ البیت جدار، البيت سقف یا السقف جدار یا الجدار سقف نہیں کہہ سکتے ۔ (۲) اجزاء ذ ہنیہ نشئی کی اجزاء ھنیہ وہ کہلاتی ہیں جو اپنے جعل و وجود میں ذات کے ساتھ متحد ہوتی ہے ذات پر محمول ہوتی ہے بلکہ اجزاء ذهنیہ میں سے ہر جزو ایک دوسرے پر محمول ہوتی ہے جیسے کہ انسان کے لئے حیوان ناطق اجزاء ذهنیہ ہیں اس لئے حیوان و ناطق انسان کے ساتھ متحد ہیں ، اس پر محمول بھی ہوتے ہیں بلکہ ہر ایک جز دوسرے پر محمول ہوتا ہے چنانچہ الانسان حیوان ناطق ، الانسان حیوان الحیوان ناطق ، الناطق حیوان کہنا صحیح ہے۔ اجزاء خارجیہ و ذهنیہ دونوں کو ایک جملہ میں یوں تعبیر کر سکتے ہو کہ مادہ وصورت اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں اور جنس وفصل اجزاء ذهنیہ کہلاتے ہیں اور جنس و فصل سے جو تعریف ہوتی ہے وہ تعریف حدی کہلاتی ہے۔

اعتراض کی تقریر :۔ یہ ہے کہ علوم مدونہ جیسے نحو وصرف، اصول فقہ وغیرہ کی حقیقتیں یا تو اس علم مدون کے مسائل مخصوصہ ہیں یا تصدیقات بالمسائل ہیں، خواہ اس کی حقیقت مسائل مخصوصہ ہو یا تصدیقات بالمسائل بہر دو صورت وہ علم مدون اجزاء غیر محمولہ (اجزاء خارجیہ) سے مرکب ہوتا ہے کیونکہ مسائل مخصوصہ یا ادراکات مسائل حمل اس علم مدون کے اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں اس لئے مسائل میں سے کسی ایک مسئلہ کا اس علم پر عمل صحیح نہیں نہ ایک مسئلہ کا دوسرے مسئلہ پر حمل صحیح ہے چنانچہ اصول الفقہ پر مثلاً الامر للوجوب کا یا الامر لوجوب پر مثلا انھی لتحریم کاحمل نہیں صحیح ہے جس کا مفاد یہ نکلتا ہے کہ علوم کے حقائق و مسائل علوم کے اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں ذہنیہ نہیں لہذا مقدمہ میں جو مفہومات کلیہ یعنی فن کی تعریف جیسے اصول فقہ کی تعريف هو علم بقواعد يتوصل بها الى الاحكام الفقهية بالادلة سے کی جاتی ہے وہ تعریف رسمی کہلا ئینگی حدی نہیں ہو سکتی ۔
حدی نہ ہونے کی دو وجہیں ہیں۔ وجہ اول :- حد علم ہے مقدمہ بنے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا، کیونکہ شئی کی تعریف محمدہ اسکی حقیقت و ماهیت کے ذریعے ہوتی ہے اور علوم مدونہ کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل مخصوصہ ہے یا مسائل مخصوصہ کی تصدیق۔ اگر علوم مدونہ کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل مخصوصہ کی تصدیق ہے تو مقدمہ میں مذکور علم کی تعریف علم کے مسائل مخصوصہ کی نفس حقیقت ہوگی ۔ پس اگر حد علم کا مقدمہ بنا صیح ہوتو ایک ہی شی کافن سے خارج ہونا اور ایک ہی شئی کافن میں داخل ہونا دونوں ہونا لازم آئے گا۔ مقدمہ چونکہ حقیقت فن سے خارج ہوتا ہے اس لئے بحیثیت مقدمہ اس کا خروج لازم آئے گا اور اگر اس مقدمہ میں حد ذکر کیا جائے تو بحیثیت حدیت دخول لازم آئے گا۔ گزر چکا کہ حد علم بالمسائل کو کہتے ہیں اور یہی علم کی حقیقت ہے پس حد علم حقیقت علم ، بلکہ نفس علم ہو جائے گا۔
بلکہ توقف الشئی علی نفسہ بھی لازم آئے گا وہ اسطرح کہ مقدمہ نام ہے جس پر علم کا شروع کرنا موقوف ہو ، پس مقدمہ ، موقوف علیہ اور علم موقوف ہوئے اور مقدمہ جو موقوف علیہ ہے اس میں حد علم یعنی علم کی حقیقت و مسائل مخصوصہ کا ذکر ہے اور علم جو موقوف ہے وہ بھی مسائل ہی ہیں غرض موقوف عالیہ بھی مسائل اور موقوف بھی مسائل ہو گئے اور یہی تو قف الشئی علی نفسہ ہے۔ پھر موقوف علیہ چونکہ موقوف کے لئے علت ہوتا ہے اور علت و معلول چونکہ نفس شئی ہے تو علیہ الشئی بنفسہ بھی لازم آئے گا
Introduction: It consists of two points. First: The preface on which the beginning of the science and art depends, which includes the definition, subject, goal and purpose of the art, that preface is external to the art, as has been verified in its place. Otherwise, the objection of 'the thing's dependence on itself' would arise. Second: The real parts of a thing are those to which the thing is in need for its subsistence, or that which is the cause of the thing's constitution; that cause is called the real parts of the thing. Then the real parts are of two types: external parts and mental parts. (1) External parts of a thing: They are called external parts which in their constitution and existence are not united with the essence, nor are they predicated on the essence, nor is any part of the external parts predicated on another. For example, for a house, separate different external parts: they are not predicated on the house, nor is it correct to predicate one of the wall and roof on the other. Thus one cannot say 'the house is a wall', 'the house is a roof', or 'the roof is a wall', or 'the wall is a roof'. (2) Mental parts of a thing: They are called mental parts which in their constitution and existence are united with the essence, are predicated on the essence, rather each mental part is predicated on another. For example, for man, 'animal' and 'rational' are mental parts. Thus 'animal' and 'rational' are united with man, are predicated on him, and each part is predicated on the other. So it is correct to say 'man is a rational animal', 'man is an animal', 'the animal is rational', 'the rational is animal'. Both external and mental parts can be expressed in one phrase: matter and form are external parts, while genus and specific difference are called mental parts, and the definition made by genus and specific difference is called a real definition (حدی).

Statement of the objection: The realities of codified sciences such as grammar, morphology, principles of jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) etc. are either the specific issues of that codified science or the intellectual perceptions of the issues. Whether its reality is specific issues or intellectual perceptions of the issues, in both cases that codified science is composed of non-predicable parts (external parts), because the specific issues or the perceptions of issues are the external parts of that codified science. Therefore, it is not correct to predicate any one issue on that science, nor is the predication of one issue on another correct. For example, it is not correct to predicate 'the command is for obligation' on usul al-fiqh, nor 'the command is for obligation' on 'the prohibition is for prohibition'. The implication is that the realities and issues of sciences are external parts, not mental. Hence, the general concepts mentioned in the preface, like the definition of the art — such as the definition of usul al-fiqh: 'it is knowledge of rules by which one arrives at legal rulings from detailed evidences' — that definition will be called a descriptive definition (رسمی), not a real definition (حدی). There are two reasons why it cannot be a real definition. First reason: A real definition is a definition; it is not fit to be a preface. Because a thing's comprehensive definition is through its reality and essence, and the reality of codified sciences is its specific issues or the verification of specific issues. If the reality of codified sciences is the verification of specific issues, then the definition of the science mentioned in the preface would be the very reality of the specific issues of the science. So if the real definition of the science were made a preface, then it would necessitate that one and the same thing be both external to the art and internal to the art. The preface, since it is external to the reality of the art, necessitates its being external as a preface; but if in this preface a real definition is mentioned, then as a definition it necessitates its being internal. It has passed that a real definition is definition by the issues, and that is the reality of the science. Thus the real definition becomes the reality of the science, rather the very science. Rather, the thing's dependence on itself would also be necessary, as follows: The preface is the name for that on which the beginning of the science depends. Thus the preface is the thing depended upon (موقوف علیہ) and the science is the dependent thing (موقوف). The preface, which is the thing depended upon, contains the real definition of the science, i.e., the reality and specific issues of the science; and the science, which is dependent, is also the issues. So the thing depended upon is also issues, and the dependent is also issues, and this is the thing's dependence on itself. Then, since the thing depended upon is a cause for the dependent, and cause and effect are the same thing, the thing's dependence on itself also follows.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational objection in usul al-fiqh concerning the definition of sciences. The author first presents a preliminary distinction between two types of parts that constitute a thing: external parts (اجزاء خارجیہ) and mental parts (اجزاء ذهنیہ). External parts are not predicated on the whole or on each other (e.g., the walls and roof of a house are external parts of the house; you cannot say 'the house is a wall'). Mental parts are predicated on the whole and on each other (e.g., 'animal' and 'rational' are mental parts of 'man'; you can say 'man is an animal' and 'the animal is rational').

Based on this, the deniers of real definitions (منکرین حد) object: A codified science like usul al-fiqh consists of its specific issues (مسائل مخصوصہ) or the mental perception of those issues. These issues are not predicable on the science itself (you cannot say 'Usul al-fiqh is the command for obligation'), nor are they predicable on each other. Therefore, they are external parts, not mental parts. Consequently, any definition of a science given in its preface can only be a descriptive definition (رسمی), not a real definition (حدی). A real definition would require the defining terms to be united with the defined object in the way mental parts are united, but that is not the case here.

The objection provides two reasons why a real definition cannot be given in the preface: (1) If the definition were real, it would be both external (as preface) and internal (as part of the science), causing contradiction. (2) It would lead to circularity (توقف الشئی علی نفسہ): the preface (which contains the definition) is supposed to be the foundation on which the science depends, but if the definition itself is the reality of the science, then the foundation and the science become identical, leading to self-dependence.

This objection sets the stage for the Hanafi response, which typically defends the possibility of a real definition by clarifying the nature of codified sciences and the role of definitions. In Hanafi usul, the definition of a science is often considered a 'حد رسمی' (descriptive definition) for practical purposes, but the dispute lies in whether it captures the true essence. This passage reflects a key debate in the philosophy of Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
اجزاء خارجیہ (ajzā' khārijiyya): Parts of a thing that are not united with the essence in existence and are not predicated on the whole or on each other.
اجزاء ذهنیہ (ajzā' dhihniyya): Parts of a thing that are united with the essence and are predicated on the whole and on each other (e.g., genus and specific difference).
حدی (ḥaddī): A real definition composed of genus and specific difference, capturing the essence.
رسمی (rasmī): A descriptive definition that does not capture the true essence but distinguishes the thing by accidental properties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection presented, why cannot the definition of a codified science be a real definition (حدی)?
A) Because the definition would be external to the science yet part of it, leading to contradiction.
B) Because the issues of the science are mental parts and cannot be defined.
C) Because the definition must be in terms of genus and specific difference, which do not exist for sciences.
D) Because the preface is part of the science, so the definition would be self-referential.

Key Takeaway
The deniers' objection rests on the claim that a science's issues are external parts, making real definition impossible; the Hanafi response must refute this classification.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

12 min
Objections to Defining a Science by Its Problems and Their Responses
Explaining two objections to definition by external parts and their refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why defining a science by its external parts (problems) fails as a proper definition (hadd) and how the Hanafi tradition resolves the objections through the distinctions between conceptual and assenting perception and the principle of consideration (i'tibar).

Core Idea
A proper definition requires predicable parts; external parts like a science's problems can only yield a description (rasm).

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut tackles a foundational issue in Hanafi usul: how to define a discipline like usul al-fiqh. Two objections are raised against defining a science by its specific problems—circularity and that the problems are non-predicable external parts. The responses clarify that conceptual knowledge can be a premise while assenting knowledge is the goal, and that the distinction between predicable and non-predicable parts is purely by consideration, allowing a unified essence to have multiple aspects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Extract the two main objections to defining a science by its specific problems: (1) circularity – knowing the problems presupposes knowing the science; (2) non-predicability – the problems are external parts that cannot serve as genus and differentia, like units of ten.
The first objection is about epistemological dependence; the second is about logical constitution.
2
Analyze the response to the first objection
Notice how the response distinguishes between two kinds of perception: conceptual (tasawwur) and assenting (tasdiq). The conceptual perception of problems is the premise, while the assenting knowledge is the goal, thus avoiding circularity.
This resolution relies on the idea that the same thing (the problems) can be considered from two different epistemic angles.
3
Analyze the response to the second objection
Understand that the difference between predicable and non-predicable parts is merely by consideration (i'tibar). The same real parts can be viewed under different conditions (bi sharṭ shay', bi sharṭ lā shay', lā bi sharṭ shay'), allowing them to function both as external parts and as predicable genus/differentia without entailing a multiplicity of essences.
This is a sophisticated logical move that preserves the unity of the science while permitting a proper definition.
4
Apply the principle of i'tibar
Practice by considering an example: the parts 'animal' and 'rational' are predicable of 'human' when considered unconditionally (lā bi sharṭ shay'), but when considered as external parts (bi sharṭ lā shay'?), they are not predicable. The key is that the same essence can have multiple considerations without contradiction.
This principle is central to Hanafi logic and usul.

ظاہر البطلان ہے لہذا مقدمہ میں مذخور تعریف کا حد ہونا باطل ہو گیا اور اگر علوم مدونہ کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل مخصوصہ میں تو مقدمہ میں مذکور تعریف کے حد ہونے کی تقدیر پرفن کے مسائل کا شروع کرنا فن کے مسائل کے جانے پر موقوف ہو جائے گا۔ وجہ دوم : ۔ مقدمہ میں بطلان حدیت کی دوسری وجہ مصنف نے اپنے قول۔ بناء على أن المركب من اجزاء غير محمولة كالعشرة لاجنس له ولا فصل له والالزم تعدد الذاتی سے بیان کی ہے۔ اس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ علم کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل مخصوصہ یا ادراکات مسائل ہوتے ہیں جن سے علم مرکب ہے اور یہ مسائل یا ادراکات اسی علم کے اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں کہ اس علم پر محمول نہیں ہوتے ہیں مثلاً الامر للوجوب اصول ذہ کا ایک مسئلہ ہے جو اصول فقہ پر محمول نہیں ہے چونکہ یہ جزء خارجی ہے اور جزء خارجی محمول نہیں ہوتا لہذا اس میں جنس و فصل بھی نہ ہوگا۔ اور جس میں جنس وفصل نہ ہو وہ حد نہیں ہوسکتا۔ مثال کے طور پر یوں سمجھو کہ عشرہ ایک عدد ہے جو دس واحد سے مرکب ہے، اور یہ وحدات عشرہ کے اجزاء خارجیہ ہیں کہ وحدات میں سے کوئی ایک بھی عشرہ پر محمول نہیں ہوتا چنانچہ العشرة واحدة ، الواحد عشرة دونوں جملے غلط ہیں پس جب اجزاء عشرہ اپنے مجموعہ مرکب پر محمول نہیں ہو سکتے تو اس میں جنس و فصل نہ ہوگی اور جو جنس و فصل سے خالی ہو حد نہیں ہو سکتی۔ اسی طرح مسائل مخصوصہ علم کے اجزاء خارجیہ ہوتے ہیں لہذا اس میں جنس و فصل کا تصور نہیں ہو سکتا بنا بریں جو تعریف مقدمہ میں ذکر کی جاتی ہے رسم ہوگی حد نہیں ہوسکتی۔ ورنہ تعد دذاتی لازم آئے گا۔ ا ا تعدد ذاتی:۔ باطل ہے اور تعدد ذاتی اس طرح لازم آئے گا کہ علم کے لئے اجزاء خارجیہ کے علاوہ اگر بطور حدیث اسی علم کے لئے جنس و فصل بھی ہو تو ایک ذات متحد متعین کے لئے دو طرح کی ذاتیات ہوئی لازم آئینگی مثلاً اصول فقہ کے لئے اجزاء خارجیہ اس کے مسائل مخصوصہ یا ادراکات مسائل تو پہلے ہی سے ثابت ہیں پھر اگر اسی علم کے لئے بطور حد جنس و فصل کو اجزاء محمولہ مانے جائیں تو ایک ہی ذات کے لئے دو طرح کے اجزاء محمولہ وغیر محمولہ ہونا لازم آئے گا اور تعدد ذاتیات بدلبتہ باطل ہے۔ بلکہ تعدد ذاتی کے ساتھ تعد وذات بھی لازم آئے گا کیونکہ دو زاتی میں سے ایک اس ذات کے قوام کے لئے اگر کافی ہے تو دوسری سے استغناء لازم ہوگا اور شئی کا اپنی ذاتی سے مستغنی ہونا غیر معقول لہذ اماننا پڑے گا کہ اس ذاتی کے لئے بھی ایک الگ ذات و حقیقت ہے اور یہ تعددذات ہے جبکہ ایک ہی ذات فرض کی گئی تھی ۔ جواب وجہ اول: بعض اعاظم نے یہ جواب دیا ہے کہ مسائل کے لئے دوطرح کے ادراک ہوتے ہیں ایک ادراک تصوری۔ کیونکہ تصور ہرشئی سے متعلق ہوتا ہے اس لئے مسائل سے بھی متعلق ہوگا دوسرا ادراک تصدیقی ۔ پس جب ادراک کی دوصورت ہوتی ہے تو ایسا ہوسکتا ہے کہ علم تصوری کے عتبار سے مقدمہ اور موقوف علیہ ہو، اور علم تصدیقی کے اعتبار سے مقصود و موقوف ہو تو کوئی اشکال نہیں لازم آتا۔ خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ مسائل کا تصور مفہوم کلی کے ذریعہ مقدمہ ہے اور خارج حقیقت ہے اور مسائل کا علم تصدیقی مقصود بالذات اور داخل حقیقت ہے اس لئے اب تو قف الشئی علی نفسہ کا اعتراض وارد نہ ہوگا کہ مقدمہ بہت تصوری ہے اور مقصود بہت تصدیقی ہے۔ قوله وفيه نظر اشرت اليه في السلم جواب وجہ دوم : ۔ اس عبارت میں منع حدیت کی وجہ دوم کے جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے جسکی طرف ماتن نے اپنی معرکۃ الآراء تصنیف ”سلم العلوم میں اشارہ کیا ہے خود حاشیہ میں بھی اس نظر و جواب کی تصریح کی ہے۔ فرماتے ہیں۔ حاصل ماذكر فيه أن الفرق بين الاجزاء المحمولة والغير المحمولة انما هو بالاعتبار ولا يمتنع تعدد الاعتبارات لشئى واحد بالذات فاللازم تعدد الذاتي بالاعتبار وهو ليس بمحال الجلسة مصنف کی پیش کردہ نظر کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اجزاء محمولہ اور اجزاء غیر محمولہ بالذات متحد ہوتے ہیں نفس الامر میں ان دونوں میں تفاوت نہیں البتہ محمولہ و غیر محمولہ میں اعتباری فرق ہے چونکہ اشیاء کے تین اعتبارات ہوتے ہیں۔ (۱) بشرط تئی ۔ (۲) بشرط لاشئی ۔ (۳) لا بشرط شی۔ اس کو مثال سے یوں سمجھو کہ انسان کے اجزائے محمولہ، حیوان و ناطق ہیں پس ان میں سے
It is clearly invalid; therefore the definition mentioned in the introduction being a proper definition becomes invalid. And if the reality of the organized sciences is in their specific problems, then on the assumption that the definition mentioned in the introduction is a proper definition, beginning the problems of the discipline would depend on knowing the problems of the discipline. Second objection: The author expressed the second reason for the invalidity of being a proper definition by his saying: 'based on the fact that a composite of non-predicable parts, like ten, has no genus and no differentia, and it necessitates multiplicity of essential property.' The summary of this is that the reality of a science is its specific problems or the perceptions of problems from which the science is composed, and these problems or perceptions are external parts of that science that are not predicated of that science. For example, 'al-amr li al-wujub' is a problem of usul al-fiqh that is not predicated of usul al-fiqh. Since it is an external part, and an external part is not predicated, therefore it will have no genus and differentia. And that which has no genus and differentia cannot be a proper definition. As an example, understand that 'ten' is a number composed of ten units, and these units are external parts of ten such that none of the units is predicated of ten. Thus both sentences 'ten is one' and 'one is ten' are false. So when the parts of ten cannot be predicated of their composite whole, it will have no genus and differentia, and that which is devoid of genus and differentia cannot be a proper definition. Similarly, the specific problems are external parts of the science, therefore no genus and differentia can be conceived in it. Hence the definition mentioned in the introduction will be a description (rasm), not a proper definition (hadd). Otherwise, multiplicity of essential property would be entailed. Multiplicity of essential property is invalid, and it would be entailed as follows: if besides the external parts, the science also had genus and differentia as parts of its definition, then for one unified determinate essence there would be two kinds of essential properties. For example, for usul al-fiqh the external parts are its specific problems or perceptions of problems and are already established. Then if for the same science, as a definition, genus and differentia are considered predicable parts, then for one essence there would have to be two kinds of parts: predicable and non-predicable. And multiplicity of essential properties is necessarily invalid. Rather, multiplicity of essential property would also necessitate multiplicity of essence, because if one of the two essential properties is sufficient for the constitution of that essence, then independence from the other would be necessary, and a thing being independent of its essential property is unintelligible. Therefore we must believe that this second essential property also has a separate essence and reality, and this is multiplicity of essence, whereas a single essence was assumed. Answer to the first objection: Some great scholars have answered that there are two kinds of perception of problems: one is conceptual perception, because conception pertains to everything, so it will pertain to problems; the second is assenting perception. So when there are two forms of perception, it is possible that the science is the premise and that on which it depends from the perspective of conceptual knowledge, and from the perspective of assenting knowledge it is the intended goal and dependent, so no problem arises. The summary is that the conception of problems through a universal concept is the premise and outside the reality, and the assenting knowledge of problems is the intended goal and inside the reality. Therefore the objection of a thing being dependent on itself will not arise, because the premise is conceptual and the intended is assenting. His saying: 'And in it there is a consideration that I pointed out in al-Sullam' is the answer to the second objection. This phrase indicates the answer to the second reason for preventing definition, which the author pointed to in his work 'Sullam al-'Ulum', and in the gloss he clarified this consideration and answer. He says: The summary of what is mentioned there is that the difference between predicable and non-predicable parts is only by consideration, and multiplicity of considerations for one thing in essence is not impossible. Therefore the necessary consequence is multiplicity of essential property by consideration, and that is not impossible. The summary of the consideration presented by the author is that predicable and non-predicable parts are united in essence; in the reality there is no difference between them. However, there is a difference in consideration between predicable and non-predicable, since things have three considerations: (1) conditioned by something, (2) conditioned by nothing, (3) unconditioned. Understand this by example: the predicable parts of man are 'living being' and 'rational'. Therefore among them...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut addresses a critical issue in the Hanafi usul tradition: the definition of a science ('ilm). The discussion arises from the claim that a science is defined by its specific problems (masa'il) or the perceptions of those problems. The author presents two objections to this being a proper definition (hadd) as opposed to a mere description (rasm). The first objection is circularity: if the science is defined by its problems, then knowing the problems requires knowing the science, leading to a vicious circle. The second objection is that the problems are external parts (ajza' kharijiyya) that cannot be predicated of the science, just as the units of ten cannot be predicated of ten. A proper definition requires predicable parts that can serve as genus and differentia; without them, no definition is possible. The author then provides responses. For the first objection, the distinction between conceptual (tasawwuri) and assenting (tasdiqi) knowledge resolves the circularity: the conceptual perception of problems is the premise, while the assenting knowledge is the goal. For the second objection, the key point is that the distinction between predicable and non-predicable parts is merely by consideration (i'tibar). The same real parts can be considered under different conditions (bi sharṭ shay', bi sharṭ lā shay', lā bi sharṭ shay'). Thus there is no impossibility in having multiple essential properties for the same essence when considered from different perspectives. This nuanced discussion reflects the Hanafi commitment to precise logical and terminological analysis in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A proper definition consisting of genus and differentia that fully captures the essence of a thing.
رسم (rasm): A description that characterizes a thing without fully capturing its essence, often using non-essential properties.
اجزاء محمولہ (ajzā' maḥmūla): Parts that can be predicated of the whole, serving as genus and differentia in a definition.
اجزاء غیر محمولہ (ajzā' ghayr maḥmūla): Parts that cannot be predicated of the whole, such as the units of ten or the problems of a science.
اجزاء خارجیہ (ajzā' khārijiyya): External parts that constitute the thing but are not logically predicable of it.
تعدد ذاتی (ta'addud dhātī): Multiplicity of essential properties, considered invalid if it implies multiple essences for one thing.
اعتبار (i'tibār): A consideration or perspective from which a thing is viewed, allowing multiple aspects without contradiction.
ادراک تصوری (idrāk taṣawwurī): Conceptual perception: grasping the meaning without judgment.
ادراک تصدیقی (idrāk taṣdīqī): Assenting perception: grasping the truth of a proposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why can the specific problems of a science not serve as its proper definition (hadd)?
A) Because the problems are too numerous to be encompassed by a definition.
B) Because the problems are external parts that cannot be predicated of the science, like the units of ten cannot be predicated of ten.
C) Because the problems are unknown before studying the science, causing circularity.
D) Because the problems are contingent and do not reflect the essence of the science.

Key Takeaway
A proper definition (hadd) requires predicable parts; defining a science by its problems yields only a description (rasm), but the principle of i'tibar allows the same parts to be considered as predicable, resolving the objections.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Problem of Unity of Conception and Assent in Definitions
Refuting the view that definitions unite conception and assent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school insists on a real disparity between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), and apply this to refute the view that definitions can unify them.

Core Idea
Definitions are conceptions; applying them to assents makes conception and assent one — impossible.

This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut Sharh Musallam al-Thubut tackles a foundational logical problem in usul al-fiqh: if the sciences consist of assents (tasdiqat), but definitions (hudud) are conceptions (tasawwurat), then defining a scientific issue would unite the two, which is impossible because they are mutually exclusive types. The author first explains how universals can be considered as genus, species, or differentia depending on the mode of consideration, making definitions possible for the issues of the sciences. However, this very possibility leads to the unity problem. The passage then refutes later logicians who argue the disparity is only specific, affirming the Hanafi view of real disparity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the modes of consideration (i'tibar)
The passage explains that the same universal (e.g., 'animal') can be genus, species, or matter depending on how it is considered: with condition (bi sharṭ shay'), without condition (la bi sharṭ), or with condition of absence (bi sharṭ la shay'). This logical framework is essential for constructing definitions.
Focus on the three modes: bi sharṭ shay', bi sharṭ la shay', la bi sharṭ.
2
Identify the definition (hadd) as a conception
A definition is composed of genus and differentia and is essentially identical to the defined (al-hadd 'ayn al-mahdud). Since definitions are concepts, they belong to the realm of conception (tasawwur), not assent (tasdiq).
Recall: conception is simple mental representation without judgment; assent includes judgment (true/false).
3
Recognize the unity problem
If the sciences (as perceptions of issues) are assents, and we take universal concepts as definitions of those sciences, then definition (conception) and defined (assent) become essentially one. This would entail unity of conception and assent, which is impossible because they are mutually exclusive types (mutabayinan).
The passage presents this as a necessary consequence on the assumption that definitions are accepted.
4
Analyze the refutation of later logicians
Later logicians (muta'akhkhirin) argue that the disparity is only at the level of specific attributes (sinafi), not real (haqiqi), similar to how Habashi and Naji are both humans despite being different subclasses. The author refutes this by insisting the disparity is real, not merely specific. The Hanafi position is that conception and assent are essentially different types.
This refutation is signalled by the word 'tahqiqan' in the matn.

قوله تحقیقا: ۔ ماتن نے اپنے قول " تحقیق سے متاخرین مناطقہ پر رد کیا ہے جو اس بات کے قائل ہیں کہ تصور و تصدیق حقیقت کے اعتبار سے ایک دوسرے کے مبائن نہیں ہیں بلکہ نوع کے اعتبار سے دونوں متحد ہیں اور لوازم و متعلقات کے اعتبار سے متبائن ہیں اور جو چیزیں لوازم صنفیہ کے اعتبار سے مبائن ہوتی ہیں وہ نوعی حیثیت سے مبائن نہیں ہوتی جیسے حبشی و نجی اپنے صنف کے اعتبار سے ایک دوسرے کے مبائن ہیں مگر نوعی لحاظ سے دونوں متحد ہیں کہ انسان ہیں ۔ مصنف کا اشارہ ہے
کر تصور و تصدیق کے مابین تباین حقیقی ہے صنفی نہیں ہے كماهوا المقرر في موضعه
In Ashraf al-Nu'ut Sharh Musallam al-Thubut, page 185: Regarding 'animal': if considered in the first mode, as a condition of being a 'natiq' (speaking), then this animal is a species; if in the second mode, as a condition of not being 'natiq', then this animal is called matter; and if in the third mode, without condition of 'natiq' and without condition of its absence, then it is genus. Similarly, 'natiq': if considered with the condition of being 'hayawan' (animal), then 'natiq' is a species; if with the condition of not being 'hayawan', then it is a form; and if without condition of 'hayawan' and its absence, then 'natiq' is a differentia. In short, non-predicable parts in the position of being considered as themselves become predicable parts when they are considered in the status of being with condition of nothing else. But if these non-predicable parts are assumed with condition of something, they become predicable parts and become genus and differentia. Therefore, the issues (masa'il) that are mentioned in the sciences, even if they are non-predicable parts, when they are considered without condition, they become predicable parts. So it is not correct to say that what is composed of non-predicable parts has no genus and differentia. Rather, the correct position is that when those issues are considered without condition, genus and differentia are conceivable for them, and when genus and differentia are conceivable, then a definition (hadd) of it is possible. Yes, in the case of accepting a definition, an important defect will arise: that conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) will become united, while they are two mutually exclusive types. The author pointed to this in his following statement: 'Yes, it necessitates the unity of conception and assent.' Explaining the above statement, the matn (text) said in the margin: 'It necessitates, on the assumption that those universal concepts are definitions of the science, the unity of conception and assent, because the definition is identical to the defined, and this if the sciences are the perceptions of the issues. But if their realities are the issues themselves, then that is built on the unity of knowledge and the known.' The explanation of the view mentioned in the text and margin is: The sciences are the name for the perceptions of issues or for the issues themselves. If sciences are the name for the perceptions of issues, i.e., the assent-based issues, then on the assumption that universal concepts are definitions, the unity of conception and assent becomes necessary, because the definition and the defined are essentially one; however, from the perspective of detail and summary they are different. It is clear that definition is of the type of conceptions, and the defined (which are the perceptions of issues) are from assents. When definition is of the type of conceptions, and the defined are assents, and definition and defined are essentially one, it follows that conception and assent become one, while they are two mutually exclusive types. This talk was on the branch when by 'sciences' the perceptions of issues are meant. But if by 'sciences' the issues themselves are meant, then the unity of conception and assent will follow in that the issues themselves are unified with the perceptions of issues because knowledge and known are unified. And since perceptions of issues refer to assents of issues, the issues themselves are also assents, because the issues themselves and the perceptions of issues are unified due to the unity of knowledge and known. Then definitions which are composed of genus and differentia are unified with the sciences, i.e., the defined, because definition and defined are essentially one. The summary: Definitions are unified with the sciences and issues, due to the essential unity of definition and defined; and issues are unified with perceptions of issues (assents of issues) due to the essential unity of knowledge and known. So definitions became unified with perceptions of issues, because the unifier of the unified is unified. And it is clear that definitions are conceptions, and perceptions of issues are assents; therefore the unity of conception and assent necessarily follows, while in reality the two types are mutually exclusive. His statement 'tahqiqan': The matn by his statement 'tahqiqan' refutes the later logicians (muta'akhkhirin) who claim that conception and assent are not mutually exclusive in reality, but are unified in terms of type, and are mutually exclusive in terms of associated particulars; and things that are mutually exclusive in terms of specific attributes are not mutually exclusive in terms of type, like 'Habashi' and 'Naji'? are mutually exclusive in terms of their class, but in terms of type they are both human. The author indicates that the disparity between conception and assent is real (haqiqi), not specific (sinafi), as established in its place.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, addressing the logical structure of definitions (hudud) in the context of usul al-fiqh. The author first explains how the same universal concept (e.g., 'animal' or 'rational') can be considered as different logical components (genus, species, differentia) depending on the mode of consideration (with condition, without condition, etc.). This establishes that definitions (composed of genus and differentia) are possible for the issues (masa'il) of the sciences when considered without condition. However, a serious problem arises: if the sciences are taken as perceptions (idrakat) of issues (which are assents), then the definition (a conception) and the defined (an assent) become essentially united, which is impossible because they are mutually exclusive types (conception vs. assent). The author then considers an alternative: if sciences are the issues themselves, then via the unity of knowledge and the known, the same problem persists. The passage concludes by refuting the later logicians who argue that the disparity is only at the level of specific attributes, not real. The author insists the disparity is real, not merely specific. This discussion is crucial for understanding how definitions function in the Islamic sciences, particularly in fiqh where universal concepts are used to define specific rulings.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A definition composed of genus and differentia that is essentially identical to the defined.
تصور (tasawwur): Conception; a mental representation of an object without judgment.
تصدیق (tasdiq): Assent; a judgment about an object, true or false.
جنس (jins): A universal predicate answering 'what is it?' in a general sense, e.g., animal.
فصل (fasl): A specific differentia distinguishing a species from others, e.g., rational.
نوع (naw'): A species under a genus, e.g., human as rational animal.
جزء غیر محمول (juz' ghayr mahmul): A part that is not predicated by itself, but becomes predicable when considered without condition.
جزء محمول (juz' mahmul): A predicable part, i.e., a universal that can be a predicate.
متبائن (mutabayin): Mutually exclusive; two things that cannot be the same type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem arises if universal concepts are taken as definitions of sciences that are assents?
A) The definitions become too broad.
B) Conception and assent become essentially united, which is impossible.
C) The sciences become indeterminate.
D) The definitions lose their genus-differentia structure.

Key Takeaway
Definitions (hudud) are conceptions and cannot be applied to assents without causing a logical contradiction; the Hanafi school affirms a real disparity between conception and assent, rejecting the later logicians' view of mere specific difference.

The Linguistic Nature of Science Names: Generic or Proper?
Presents ikhtilāf on science names: generic or proper?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi views on the classification of science names and apply the refutation using the 'house' example to distinguish generic nouns from proper nouns.

Core Idea
Names of sciences are generic nouns (ism jins), not proper nouns, as established by necessity.

In this passage from Muslim al-Thubut, a dispute (ikhtilāf) is presented about whether the names of sciences like fiqh and kalam are generic nouns that apply to all their instances, or proper nouns that designate the entire field as a unique entity. Three positions are recorded, with the first—generic nouns—deemed the apparent view (al-zāhir). The commentator refutes the third position by drawing an analogy to the word 'house' (bayt), which is a generic noun despite not applying to its individual parts. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the logical refutation, helping you understand how generic nouns function in legal language and why this matters for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the passage and list the three positions: (1) generic nouns (asmā' jins), (2) proper nouns for the genus (a'lām jins), (3) proper nouns for individuals (a'lām shakhsiyyah). Note which one is called 'al-zāhir'.
The matn author prefers the first view.
2
Understand the proof for each view
For the second view, the response is 'yathbutu bi al-darūrah' (established by necessity), meaning the generic noun status is self-evident. For the third view, the proof is that fiqh does not apply to each individual issue, so it must be a proper noun for the collection.
The third view confuses non-applicability to parts with proper noun status.
3
Analyze the refutation of the third view
The commentator gives the example of 'house' (bayt): it is a generic noun even though its parts (e.g., a room) are not called 'house'. This shows that a universal concept can be composed of parts without applying to each part. Thus the lack of applicability does not necessitate proper noun status.
Also mentions that parts may be homogeneous (four) or heterogeneous (sikanjabīn).
4
Extract the underlying principle
The key principle: a generic noun can denote a whole composed of parts without applying to the parts individually. This principle preserves the generality of science names in legal discourse.
This relates to the usul concept of 'umūm' (generality) and how commands in fiqh texts apply across individual issues.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh applications
Consider how the classification affects the interpretation of commands like 'Iqīmū al-ṣalāh' (establish prayer). If 'ṣalāh' is a generic noun, the command applies to every instance of prayer. If it were a proper noun for the genus, it would be a fixed name for the species, less flexible for analogical extension.
This dispute impacts how we understand the scope of legal terms and their use in istinbāṭ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : "ثم اختلف فى أسماء العلوم، فقيل اسماء جنس وهو الظاهر وقيل بل اعلام جنس قلنا يثبت بالضرورة وقيل بل شخصيته اذلايصدق الفقه مثلا على مسألة مسألة اقول وفيه انه منقوض بالبيت والحل أن المعنى الكلى قديكون مركبا من أجزاء متفقة نحو الا ربعة او مختلفة نحو السكنجبين فلا يلزم من عدم الصدق على البعض الشخصية ترجمه :۔ پھر اسماء علوم کے بارے میں اختلاف رونما ہوا تو کہا گیا کہ یہ اسماء جنس ہیں اور یہی ظاہر ہے اور یہ کہا گیا ہے کہ یہ اسماء اعلام جنس ہیں ہم کہتے ہیں علم جنس کا ثبوت بر بنائے ضرورت ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہ قول بھی کیا گیا ہے کہ اسماء علوم اعلام شخصی ہیں کیونکہ ہر ہر مسئلہ پر مثلا فقہ صادق نہیں آتا ہے۔ میں
The matn of al-Muslim: 'Then there is disagreement concerning the names of the sciences. It is said they are generic nouns (asmā' jins), and that is the apparent view. And it is said they are proper nouns for the genus (a'lām jins). We say it is established by necessity. And it is said they are proper nouns for individuals (shakhsiyyah), because for example, fiqh does not apply to each and every individual issue. I say: This is refuted by the example of a house. The solution is that a universal meaning may be composed of parts that are similar (e.g., four) or different (e.g., vinegar-and-honey drink); so the lack of applicability to some parts does not necessitate individual proper noun status.' Translation: Then disagreement occurred regarding the names of sciences. It was said that they are generic nouns, and this is apparent. And it was said that they are proper nouns for the genus. We say that the generic noun is established by necessity. And this was also said: that the names of sciences are proper nouns for individuals because, for example, fiqh does not apply to every individual issue. I...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilāf) from the matn of Muslim al-Thubut regarding the linguistic nature of the names of sciences (e.g., fiqh, kalam). Three positions are recorded. The first (and apparently preferred) view is that they are generic nouns (ism jins), meaning they apply to all members of a class without being fixed to a specific individual. The second view is that they are proper nouns for the genus (alam jins), i.e., they are fixed designations for the entire genus rather than being true generics. The matn author rejects this by saying 'we say it is established by necessity' (probably affirming the generic noun view). The third view is that they are proper nouns for individuals (alam shakhsi), arguing that fiqh does not apply to each individual issue (i.e., it's a name for the whole collection, not each part). The commentator refutes this with an example: 'house' is a generic noun even though parts of a house (like a room) are not called 'house'; similarly, a universal concept can be composed of parts, and the lack of applicability to parts does not make it a proper noun. He adds that parts may be homogeneous (like four) or heterogeneous (like vinegar-and-honey drink). The translation in Urdu summarizes the same. Overall, the Hanafi preference is for the first view: names of sciences are generic nouns.

Key Terms
اسم جنس (ism jins): A generic noun that applies to all individuals of a class without being fixed to one, e.g., 'insān' (human).
علم جنس (alam jins): A proper noun for the genus, a designation for the entire type as a proper name.
علم شخصی (alam shakhsi): A proper noun for a particular individual, e.g., 'Zayd'.
نقض (naqd): A counterexample used to invalidate a general claim or inference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary refutation of the view that names of sciences are proper nouns for individuals (a'lām shakhsiyyah)?
A) It violates the principle of ijma'.
B) The word 'house' is a generic noun even though it does not apply to its parts, showing that lack of applicability to parts does not imply proper noun status.
C) Sciences are abstract, so they cannot have proper nouns.
D) The Quran uses science names as generics.

Key Takeaway
Names of sciences like fiqh are generic nouns (asmā' jins), as affirmed by the preferred Hanafi view and the logical refutation using the analogy of 'house'.
