Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
Rational Good and Evil: Do They Necessitate a Divine Ruling?
Dispute on rational good/evil and divine rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi-Maturidi position on the relationship between rational good/evil and divine rulings, and evaluate the objection and responses regarding the nature of hukm.

Core Idea
Rational good and evil do not create legal obligation; they only make an action worthy of a ruling until divine address arrives.

The relationship between reason and revelation is a foundational debate in usul al-fiqh. The Mu'tazila argued that actions inherently demand rulings, even without a Lawgiver. The Hanafi-Maturidi school maintained that rulings depend on divine address, while affirming that reason can discern good and evil. In this passage, the author presents the dispute, a verbal objection, a proposed resolution, and a critique of that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first part of the passage and distinguish the Mu'tazili view (actions necessitate rulings by their inherent good/evil) from the Maturidi view (actions only make one worthy of a ruling, actual ruling requires divine address).
Note the five points of difference listed between the schools.
2
Analyze the objection
The objector claims the dispute is merely verbal (lafzi) because 'hukm' could mean either divine address (khitab) or worthiness of reward/punishment. Understand why each reading would collapse the disagreement.
If hukm = khitab, Mu'tazila cannot claim it before revelation; if hukm = worthiness, both sides agree it exists, so no dispute.
3
Examine the proposed response
Some scholars respond that 'hukm' means 'occupation of responsibility' (ishtighal al-dhimma), a mental consideration by the Lawgiver that can exist without speech. See why this redefinition aims to keep the dispute real.
Understand the concept of ishtighal al-dhimma as the Lawgiver's consideration that the servant's responsibility is occupied with an action.
4
Consider the critique
The author objects that this response contradicts the standard usul definition of hukm as divine address, and that the Lawgiver's consideration is identical to internal speech (kalam nafsi), which is eternal and inseparable from speech.
This critique shows the response is flawed and the dispute remains a real substantive disagreement.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: Mu'tazila hold people before revelation are accountable for good/evil by reason alone; Hanafis hold they are not accountable for specific rulings until revelation reaches them.
This affects the status of ahl al-fatrah (people in the interregnum) and those unreached by da'wa.

ماتریدیہ کے خلاف معتزلہ، امامیہ، کرامیہ اور براہمہ خذھم اللہ کا قول ہے کہ حسن و فتح بندہ میں حکم کو لازم کرتے ہیں بالفرض اگر شارع نہ ہوتے یا اسکا حکم ہی موجود نہ ہوتا اور افعال اور اس کے مرتکبین ہوتے تو ان کے افعال پر وجوب حرمت، اباحت و کراہت جیسے احکام لازم ہوتے لہذا وہ فعل جو وجوب کے صالح ہے واجب ہوگا، جو اباحت کے لائق ہے مباح ٹھہرے گا ، جو حرمت کو مقتضی ہے حرام ہوگا اور جو فعل کراہت کے مناسب ہے مکروہ گردانا جائے گا ، غرض ان حمقاء نے جس طرح حسن و فتح کے شرعی ہونے سے انکار کیا ہے ویسے ہی حکم کے شرعی ہونے سے بھی انکار کیا اس لئے ان لوگوں کے نزدیک مکلف ہونے کے لئے دعوت کی تبلیغ شرط نہیں ہے لہذا شاہق جبل والے اور اصل فترت افعال حسنہ کے ترک اور سیئات کے ارتکاب کے سبب معذب ہوں گے اور ترک قبائح اور امتثال حسنات کے سبب مشاب ہوں گے ۔
فرق دونوں مذاہب میں مندرجہ ذیل طریقوں سے فرق ہے۔
(۱) معتزلہ کے نزدیک فعل کا حسن و فتح نفس حکم کو واجب کرتا ہے۔

اشکال : ۔ یہاں ایک قوی اشکال وارد ہوتا ہے کہ حنفیہ ماتریدیہ اور معتزلہ کے درمیان یہ نزاع حقیقی نہیں ہے بلکہ لفظی ہے وہ اسطرح کہ زیر بحث مسئلہ میں حکم سے مراد اگر حکم کا معنی مشہور خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ المتعلق بافعال المکلفین" ہے تو اس تقدیر پر درود شرع سے پیشتر حکم کے ہونے کا قول کرنا صحیح نہیں کیونکہ اس معنی کے اعتبار سے درود شرع سے پہلے خطاب کا سوال ہی پیدا نہیں ہوتا ور نہ خطاب کا اثبات بغیر شارع کے لازم آئے گا حالانکہ خطاب کا اثبات بغیر شارع مخاطب کے تصور نہیں ہوسکتا پس معتزلہ کے حکم کے قول کو کوئی راہ نہیں اور اگر حکم سے کون افعل مناطالثواب والعقاب‘ مراد ہے یعنی فعل کا ثواب و عقاب کا مدار ہونا تو اس تقدیر پر حسن و قبح عقلی کا قول کرنے کے باوجود پھر اس قول کا کوئی معنی نہ ہوگا کہ حسن عقلی ہے اور حکم شرعی ہے اور نہ نزاع لفظی قرار پائے گا اس لئے کہ جس نے ( معتزلہ ) درود شرع سے پیشتر حکم کا قول کیا ہے اس نے حکم کا معنی ثانی مراد لیا ہے اور جس نے ورود شرع سے پیشتر حکم کی نفی کی ہے انہوں نے حکم کا معنی اول مراد لیا ہے۔
جواب: بعض اعاظم نے جواب دیا ہے کہ محل نزاع میں حکم سے مراد حکم کا معنی مشہور اول ہے نہ ثانی جیسا کہ معترض نے بیان کیا بلکہ اس جگہ حکم سے مراد ( اشتغال ذمتہ العبد بالفعل ) ہے یعنی ذمہ عبد کا فعل کے ساتھ مشغول رہنا اور اشتغال یہ ہے کہ ، شارع کا اعتبار کرنا کہ بندہ کے ذمہ میں جبر افعل ہے یا کف ہے پس جب حکم سے اشتغال ذمتہ العبد مراد ہے اور اشتغال اعتبار شارع کا نام ہے تو ایسا ہو سکتا ہے حکم ہو خطاب نہ ہو اس لئے کہ محض اعتبار کے لئے کسی خطاب یا کلام کی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی .. اور حسن و قبیح اعتبار شارع کو واجب نہیں کرتا جیسا کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک حکم کو واجب کرتے ہیں کیونکہ حسن و فتح زیادہ سے زیادہ وصول ثواب و عقاب کے لئے استعداد و صالح ہوتے ہیں ۔ رہے یہ کہ اس استعداد و صلاحیت کے اعتبار سے اعتبار شارع مذکور کا اشتغال الذمه بالفعل والکف سے متعلق ہونا تو خیال رہے کہ اعتبار شارع کا اس سے تعلق نہیں ہے لہذا حکم نہیں ۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ حسن و فتح میں نقلی ہونے کے اتفاق کے باوجود اگر حکم کا معنی اشتغال ذمتہ العبد مراد لیا جائے تو فریقین کے درمیان نزاع تحیح ہے۔
رو :۔ اس جواب میں سقم ہے اس لئے کہ حکم کا جو معنی مجیب نے بیان کیا ہے وہ معنی اصولیین سے منقول معنی کے خلاف ہے تو اصولیین کے کلام کو حکم کے اس معنی پر کیونکر محمول کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ نیز یہ کہنا کہ اعتبار شارع کے لئے کسی خطاب و کلام کی ضرورت نہیں ممنوع ہے کیونکہ خطاب نفسی اور کلام نفسی اسی اعتبار شارع کا مین ہے اور وہ قدیم ہے اور اس کا تعلق بعد شرع حادث ہوتا ہے میں خطاب اور اعتبار کا حال ایک ہی جیسا ہے گرچہ دونوں مفہوم کے اعتبار سے متغائر ہیں۔ اور بھی کئی وجہوں سے جواب
Against the Maturidiyya, the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Barahima—may Allah seize them—hold the view that good (husn) and bad (qubh) necessitate the ruling (hukm) upon the servant. Even if the Lawgiver were not or His ruling did not exist, and actions and their perpetrators existed, then obligations, prohibitions, permissions, and dislikes would be necessitated upon their actions. Therefore, an action suitable for obligation becomes obligatory, one fit for permission becomes permissible, one warranting prohibition becomes forbidden, and one appropriate for dislike becomes disliked. In short, these foolish people have denied the shar'i nature of good and bad just as they have denied the shar'i nature of the ruling. Thus, according to them, the propagation of the call is not a condition for being legally accountable; hence, the people of the mountain peak and those of original nature will be punished for abandoning good deeds and committing evil ones, and rewarded for abandoning evil deeds and following good ones.
The difference between the two schools is as follows:
(1) According to Mu'tazila, the good or bad of an act necessitates the very ruling.
(2) According to the Maturidiyya, good and bad only cause the worthiness of a ruling.
(3) According to Mu'tazila, the imposition of a ruling upon the servant does not depend on Allah's command or prohibition.
(4) According to Maturidiyya, the imposition of a ruling depends on divine command and prohibition.
(5) According to Mu'tazila, both good/bad and ruling are rational ('aqli). According to Maturidiyya, good/bad are rational but ruling is shar'i.
Objection: A strong objection arises here that this dispute between Hanafi-Maturidis and Mu'tazila is not real but purely verbal. In the issue under discussion, if by 'hukm' is meant the well-known meaning 'the address of Allah the Exalted pertaining to the actions of the legally responsible persons', then on this premise, saying that a ruling exists before the advent of the law is not correct, because in this sense, the question of address does not arise before the law, nor can address be affirmed without a lawgiver, since the affirmation of address is inconceivable without an addressing lawgiver. Thus, the Mu'tazila's claim of a ruling has no basis. And if by 'hukm' is meant 'that which makes an action a locus of reward and punishment', i.e., the action's being a basis for reward and punishment, then even with holding that good and bad are rational, their statement 'good is rational and ruling is shar'i' would have no meaning, and the dispute would not become verbal, because the one (Mu'tazila) who claimed a ruling before the law intended the second meaning, and those who denied a ruling before the law intended the first meaning.
Response: Some great scholars have answered that in the locus of dispute, by 'hukm' is meant neither the well-known first meaning nor the second, but rather 'the occupation of the servant's responsibility by the action' (ishtighal dhimmat al-'abd bi al-fi'l), i.e., the responsibility of the servant being occupied with the action. And this occupation is the lawgiver's consideration that the servant's responsibility has an obligation to do or refrain. So when hukm means occupation of responsibility, and occupation is the lawgiver's consideration, then it is possible that a ruling exists without an address, because mere consideration does not require an address or speech. And good/bad do not necessitate the lawgiver's consideration as they necessitate a ruling according to Mu'tazila, because good and bad at most are readiness and suitability for receiving reward and punishment. As for whether this readiness and suitability cause the lawgiver's aforementioned consideration of occupation of responsibility by action or refrain, note that the lawgiver's consideration has no connection to it; hence there is no ruling. Summary: Despite agreement that good and bad are shar'i (transmitted), if by hukm is meant occupation of responsibility, then the dispute between the parties is real (haqiqi).
Critique: This response is flawed because the meaning of hukm given by the respondent contradicts the transmitted meaning from the usul scholars; thus how can the words of usul scholars be interpreted upon this meaning? Also, the claim that the lawgiver's consideration does not require any address or speech is forbidden, because internal address (khitab nafsi) and internal speech (kalam nafsi) are exactly this lawgiver's consideration, and it is eternal, and its connection occurs after the advent of the law. The case of address and consideration is the same, although they are different in terms of concept. And there are many other reasons [for the response being flawed].
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a key usul dispute between the Hanafi-Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila on the relationship between rational good and evil (husn wa qubh) and divine rulings (ahkam). The author first states the Mu'tazili position: that actions themselves, by their inherent good or bad, necessitate a divine ruling (obligation, prohibition, etc.) even without revelation. They believe that rational judgment alone imposes legal obligation. The Maturidi position, which is the Hanafi doctrinal stance, holds that good and bad are rational concepts but they do not necessitate a ruling; they only make an action worthy of a ruling. The actual ruling depends on divine address (khitab). The passage then lists five points of difference between the two schools. After that, an objection (ishkal) is raised: maybe the dispute is merely verbal, since 'hukm' can be understood in two ways—either as divine address (which cannot exist before revelation) or as the property of being worthy of reward/punishment. The objector argues that if the first meaning is used, Mu'tazila cannot claim ruling before revelation; if the second, then Maturidis cannot say ruling is shar'i only. A response is given by some great scholars (ba'd al-a'zam): that 'hukm' in this dispute means 'occupation of the servant's responsibility' (ishtighal al-dhimma), which is a mental consideration by the Lawgiver and does not require speech. Hence, it can exist before revelation, and the dispute remains real. However, the author also notes a critique (ruwwa) of this response: it contradicts the usuli definition of hukm, and it falsely separates the Lawgiver's consideration from internal speech (kalam nafsi), which is eternal. Thus the response is problematic. The commentary is a typical Hanafi usul discussion on the nature of rational good and evil and their role in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Goodness of an action, which according to Hanafis is rational but does not necessitate a ruling.
قبح (qubh): Evil of an action, rational but not necessitating a ruling for Hanafis.
حكم (hukm): In this dispute, debated whether it means divine address (khitab) or occupation of responsibility (ishtighal al-dhimma) or worthiness of reward/punishment.
خطاب (khitab): Divine speech addressing the mukallaf, which according to Hanafis is necessary for the existence of a ruling.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Worthiness of reward or punishment, which Hanafis attribute to rational good/evil, as opposed to the actual ruling.
اشتغال الذمة (ishtighal al-dhimma): Occupation of the servant's responsibility, a proposed meaning for hukm in the response, defined as the Lawgiver's consideration that the servant's responsibility is engaged by an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, the objection (ishkal) suggests that the dispute between Maturidis and Mu'tazila might be merely verbal. What is the basis of this objection?
A) Both schools agree that actions have inherent moral qualities, so the disagreement is only about terminology.
B) The term 'hukm' can mean either divine address or worthiness of reward/punishment, and depending on the meaning, the dispute either disappears or becomes trivial.
C) The Mu'tazila changed their position over time, so the dispute is not consistent.
D) The Hanafi position is actually the same as the Mu'tazili position, just expressed differently.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Maturidi position is that rational good and evil make an action worthy of a ruling but do not create legal obligation; only divine address (khitab) establishes the actual ruling.

The Maturidi Hanafi Position on Intellect's Independence
Explaining the Maturidi Hanafi position and refuting conflation with Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maturidi Hanafi position on intellect's independent role in rulings, explain how it differs from the Mu'tazila and majority Hanafis, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a scenario without revelation.

Core Idea
Intellect independently perceives the goodness of faith and evilness of disbelief, but not all rulings.

In this passage from 'Ashraf al-Nu'ut', the commentator explains a disputed issue in usul al-fiqh: can human reason ('aql) independently arrive at Shari'ah rulings without revelation? The Maturidi Hanafis take a middle ground: intellect is independent in a few matters (like the obligation of faith and prohibition of disbelief), but not in detailed rulings that require Shari'a. We will explore this position, how it contrasts with the Mu'tazila and the majority Hanafis, and why the author defends it against accusations of being identical to Mu'tazilism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main positions
List the majority Hanafi view (intellect has no independent role), the Mu'tazila view (intellect entails all rulings), and the Maturidi Hanafi view (intellect entails some rulings).
The Maturidis are represented by Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and Sadr al-Shari'a.
2
Analyze the scope of each position
Note the key difference: the Maturidis limit intellect's independence to very few matters (like iman and kufr), while Mu'tazila extend it to many or all rulings. The majority Hanafis deny any independence.
The author emphasizes that conflating Maturidis with Mu'tazila is a mistake.
3
Examine the causal categories
Understand the three views on intellect's causal role: Mu'tazila see it as an efficient cause (illa mujiba), Ash'aris as a mere indicator (illa muhammira), and Maturidis as making the ruling necessary through knowledge of God, without being an efficient cause.
This is from the end of the first paragraph: 'عقل نہ علت موجبہ ہے نہ علت محمدرہ بلکہ عقل اہلبیت حکم کو اور علیم وخبیر سے تعلق محکم کو واجب کرتی ہے'.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: if someone lives isolated without revelation, the Maturidis hold they are obliged to believe and will be punished for disbelief; majority Hanafis and Ash'aris say no obligation until revelation; Mu'tazila hold them accountable for all rational duties.
This is the key difference that matters in fatwa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author supports the Maturidi view, arguing it avoids both extremes. Note that the narration from Abu Hanifa (in the next lesson) provides evidence for this position.
Tarjih is based on textual proof and rational argument.

مستلزم حکم ہے اور بعض میں مستلزم حکم نہیں ہے۔ ہماری اس تقریر سے روشن ہو گیا کہ بعض حفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں واضح فرق ہے لہذا بعض شراح مسلم کا یہ کہنا کہ بعض حنفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں کوئی فرق نہیں پھر جواب دینے کی کوشش تطویل لا طائل ہے۔ چنانچہ میرے زیر نگاہ ابھی مسلم کی تین معتمد عربی شرحیں ہیں جن میں کہا گیا ہے کہ بعض حنفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں اس مسئلہ میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے کیونکہ الاسلام کے کلام سے مفہوم ہوتا ہے کہ ماتریدیہ، معتزلہ اور اشاعرہ کے مابین حاصل نزاع یہ ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل علت موجبہ ہے حکم کے لئے اور اشعریہ کے نزدیک علت محمدرہ اور ماتریدیہ کے نزدیک عقل نہ غلت موجبہ ہے نہ علت محمد رہ بلکہ عقل اہلبیت حکم کو اور علیم وخبیر سے تعلق محکم کو واجب کرتی ہے۔
قوله وروى عن أبى حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى الخ اس عبارت سے بعض حنفیہ کے موقف کی تائید و توثیق ہوتی ہے کہ احناف کے امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ کی روایت سے ظاہر ہے کہ عقل بعض احکام کے ادراک میں مستقل ہے چنانچہ کتاب منتقلی میں، پھر امیز ان میں محمد بین ساعت عن محمد بن الحسن اور جامع الاسرار وغیرہ میں عن ابی یوسف عن ابی حنیفہ مروی ہے کہ کوئی عاقل اپنے خالق سے جاہل رہ جانے کے سبب قیامت کے دن معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا بلکہ معذب ہوگا کیونکہ وہ خالق کے وجود، اس کی وحدانیت اور اس کے صفات کمالیہ کے ثبوت پر، آسمان وز مین کی پیدائش، اختلاف لیل و نہار و دیگر مخلوقات و عجائب قدرت اور خود اپنی خلقت جیسے دلائل و شواہد کا مشاہدہ کر رہا ہے اس لئے اسمیں قطعا شک کی گنجائش نہیں۔ عقلاً معرفت الہی کو ثابت کرتے ہوئے اس جگہ فاضل خیر آبادی نے کہا ہے۔ حق یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ اور اس کے صفات پر ایمان صفت کمال ہے اور اس سے کفر جمیع عقلاء کے نزدیک صفت نقصان ہے نیز ایمان شکر نعمت ہے اور شکر نعمت صفت کمال ہے اور کفر، کفران نعمت ہے اور کفران نعمت صفت نقصان ہے پس منقل کے نزدیک ایمان صفت حسن اور کفر فتیح ہو گا اس لئے لازم ہے کر عقل حسن کی طرف رغبت رکھے اور فتح سے بے رغبتی کا اظہار کرے لہذا جو بندہ امر مستحسن عقلی کو ترک کرے گا معاقب ہوگا معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا گر چہ اللہ عز شانہ کی طرف سے کوئی حکم نہیں آیا ہو۔
His statement: 'Then among the Hanafis is one who said that intellect...' meaning: Some Hanafi scholars, such as Imam Alam al-Huda Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Imam Fakhr al-Islam Sahib al-Mizan, and Sadr al-Shari'a, etc., said that intellect, without seeking aid from the Shari'a, can independently perceive some of the rulings of Allah the Exalted due to the goodness and evilness of actions. Accordingly, these noble scholars declared it obligatory upon every mature sane person, even a sane child, whether the call of the Messenger reached him or not, to believe in Allah the Exalted, and declared forbidden all things that are not befitting the majesty of Allah the Exalted, such as injustice and foolishness, etc., because intellect has indicated the goodness of faith and the evilness of disbelief. The summary is that according to these scholars, rulings are of two types: one in the perception of which intellect is not independent, so intellect cannot impose obligation or prohibition, etc. The second is that in which intellect is independent in perceiving it without the aid of Shari'a. So intellect can impose obligation and prohibition in it. In short, the madhhab of these some Hanafis lies between the madhhab of the majority Hanafis and the madhhab of the Mu'tazila: the majority Hanafis claim that intellect absolutely does not entail a ruling, and the Mu'tazila claim that intellect absolutely entails a ruling. Separate from both of these, the madhhab of some Hanafis is that intellect entails a ruling in some matters and does not entail a ruling in others. From our exposition it becomes clear that there is a clear difference between the madhhab of some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila. Therefore, the statement of some commentators of Musallam that there is no difference between some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila, and then their attempt to answer is a lengthy fruitless endeavor. Indeed, before me now are three reliable Arabic commentaries on Musallam in which it is said that there is no difference between some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila in this issue because the Mu'tazila hold that in some matters the ruling can be perceived by intellect, not dependent on Shari'a, and some Hanafis also hold the same. However, a difference can be made between the two madhhabs in this way: the rulings regarding which some Hanafis consider intellect independent are very few, like faith and disbelief. And according to the Mu'tazila, the rulings perceived by intellect are many. Some great scholars have said, distinguishing between them, that according to the Mu'tazila, intellect in itself entails all rulings, even though intellect only perceives the goodness and evilness of some actions. And the Maturidis, although they have stated that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings, they did not say that it perceives all rulings through goodness and evilness. Nevertheless, the madhhab of some Hanafis will face the accusation of arbitrariness because they have given intellect a decisive ruling for some actions and not for others, which is mere arbitration. The essence of the dispute: From the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam it is understood that the dispute among the Maturidis, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris is that the Mu'tazila consider intellect an efficient cause (illa mujiba) for the ruling, the Ash'aris consider it an unprepared cause (illa muhammira), and the Maturidis consider it neither an efficient cause nor an unprepared cause, rather intellect makes the ruling necessary and from the relationship of the One Who Knows and is Aware to the ruling it makes it necessary.
His statement: 'And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him...' This phrase supports and confirms the position of some Hanafis. From the narration of the great Imam of the Hanafis, Abu Hanifa, it is apparent that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings. Indeed, in the book Muntala, then in... [؟] Muhammad bin... [؟] from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and in Jami' al-Asrar, etc., from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that no sane person will be considered excused on the Day of Resurrection due to being ignorant of his Creator, but rather will be punished, because he observes evidence and proofs for the existence of the Creator, His Oneness, and His attributes of perfection, such as the creation of the heavens and earth, the alternation of night and day, other creatures and wonders of power, and his own creation. Therefore, there is absolutely no room for doubt. Proving knowledge of God intellectually, at this point, the virtuous al-Khayrabadi said: The truth is that faith in Allah and His attributes is an attribute of perfection, and disbelief in Him is, according to all rational people, an attribute of deficiency. Also, faith is gratitude for blessings, and gratitude for blessings is an attribute of perfection, and disbelief is ingratitude for blessings, and ingratitude for blessings is an attribute of deficiency. So according to intellect, faith is an attribute of goodness and disbelief is an attribute of evilness. Therefore, it is necessary that intellect inclines towards goodness and shows aversion to evilness. Hence, a servant who abandons a rationally good action will be punished, not considered excused, even if no command has come from Allah, Exalted is His Glory.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage from 'Ashraf al-Nu'ut' is a commentary on 'Musallam al-Thubut', discussing the epistemological question of whether the human intellect ('aql) can independently arrive at Shari'ah rulings without revelation. The author explains that some Hanafi scholars (particularly the Maturidis: Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Sadr al-Shari'a) hold that intellect can independently perceive the moral value (husn/qubh) of some actions. They therefore rule that faith (iman) in God is obligatory upon every sane person, even a child, regardless of whether the prophetic call reached them, because intellect recognizes the goodness of faith and the evilness of disbelief. Similarly, actions like injustice are forbidden by intellect alone. These scholars distinguish between rulings that intellect can perceive (like the necessity of belief and prohibition of disbelief) and those it cannot (like details of prayer, fasting, etc.). The author then positions this view as a middle ground between the majority Hanafis (who deny any independent legal force for intellect) and the Mu'tazila (who claim intellect is the sole determinant of all rulings irrespective of revelation). He rebuts claims by some commentators of Musallam that there is no difference between these some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila, pointing out that the Hanafis limit intellect's scope to very few rulings, while the Mu'tazila extend it to many. He further distinguishes the Maturidi position from Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: Mu'tazila see intellect as an efficient cause, Ash'aris as a mere indicator, and Maturidis as making the ruling necessary through knowledge of God. The passage then provides a narration from Abu Hanifa (through chains in Muntala, Jami' al-Asrar) stating that ignorance of the Creator is no excuse because rational evidence (the universe, etc.) proves His existence and attributes. Finally, al-Khayrabadi's reasoning is quoted: faith is perfection and gratitude, disbelief is deficiency and ingratitude, so rational good must be pursued and evil avoided; hence punishment for abandoning rationally good acts even without revelation.

Key Terms
عقل ('aql): The human faculty of reason that can independently perceive the moral value (husn/qubh) of some actions and thereby derive certain Shari'ah rulings (e.g., obligation of iman, prohibition of kufr) without revelation.
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): The inherent goodness or evilness of acts as perceived by intellect; acts are not morally neutral but have intrinsic qualities that reason can discern.
علة موجبة (illa mujiba): An efficient cause that necessarily produces its effect; here, Mu'tazila view intellect as the efficient cause of rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi Hanafi position described in this lesson, how many types of rulings are there in terms of intellect's perception?
A) One: intellect perceives all rulings.
B) Two: those intellect can perceive independently and those it cannot.
C) Three: those intellect perceives, those revelation perceives, and those both.
D) None: intellect has no role.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi Hanafi position on intellect is a moderate one: intellect independently knows the obligation of faith and prohibition of disbelief, but not all rulings.

The Dalil from Abu Hanifa: Intellect as Independent Proof for Obligation of Faith
Providing the primary textual evidence from Abu Hanifa for the Maturidi position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the narration from Abu Hanifa about intellect and ignorance of the Creator, analyze its evidentiary value (thubut and dalala), and use it to support the Maturidi Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Ignorance of the Creator is no excuse because rational evidence suffices.

The first lesson laid out the Maturidi Hanafi position that intellect independently perceives the goodness of faith and evilness of disbelief. Now we examine the primary textual evidence for that position: a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa himself. This hasan hadith, transmitted through Abu Yusuf and others, states that no sane person will be excused on the Day of Resurrection for ignorance of God, because the created world provides ample rational proof. The commentator also adds a rational argument: faith is perfection, disbelief is deficiency, and intellect naturally pursues perfection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the dalil
Recognize the narration is from Abu Hanifa, transmitted through chains in Muntala, Jami' al-Asrar, etc. It is a sunnah ahad (zanni al-thubut) but qati al-dalala.
The text: 'و روى عن أبى حنيفة... أنه لا عاقل يجهل خالقه في القيامة معذورا بل يعذب'.
2
Analyze the thubut and dalala
Thubut = zanni (single narrator chain). Dalala = qati (clear in meaning: ignorance is inexcusable).
The author uses this as proof, so he considers it reliable enough.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith equates failure to know God with inexcusable ignorance, implying that the rational evidence (the universe) is sufficient to obligate belief. This supports the Maturidi view.
The phrase 'مشاهد للدلائل' indicates that observation of creation is enough.
4
Connect to the rational argument
The commentator adds a rational argument from al-Khayrabadi: faith is perfection and gratitude, disbelief is deficiency and ingratitude. Intellect naturally prefers perfection, so it obligates faith.
This is not a separate dalil but a supporting rational proof.
5
Assess the strength and objections
Consider potential objections: the narration is ahad; the rational argument may be contested by Ash'aris. The Maturidi response: the hadith is accepted by the Hanafi school, and the rational argument is sound.
The author does not discuss objections in this passage, but they are implied.

وہ کہ عقل اس کے ادراک کرنے میں مستقل ہے ورو د شرع کی استعانت کے بغیر ۔ پس عقل اس میں وجوب و حرمت وغیرھا کا حکم لگا سکتی ہے غرض یہ کہ ان بعض علماء احناف کا مذہب، مذہب جمہور احناف اور مذہب اہل اعتزال کے بین بین واقع ہے کہ جمہور احناف کا ادعاء یہ ہے کہ عقل مطلقا مستلزم حکم نہیں ہے اور معتزلہ کا ادعاء یہ ہے کہ عقل علی الاطلاق مستلزم حکم ہے اور ان دونوں سے الگ بعض حنفیہ کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ عقل بعض میں مستلزم حکم ہے اور بعض میں مستلزم حکم نہیں ہے۔ ہماری اس تقریر سے روشن ہو گیا کہ بعض حفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں واضح فرق ہے لہذا بعض شراح مسلم کا یہ کہنا کہ بعض حنفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں کوئی فرق نہیں پھر جواب دینے کی کوشش تطویل لا طائل ہے۔ چنانچہ میرے زیر نگاہ ابھی مسلم کی تین معتمد عربی شرحیں ہیں جن میں کہا گیا ہے کہ بعض حنفیہ اور معتزلہ کے مذہب میں اس مسئلہ میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے کیونکہ معتزلہ قائل ہیں کہ بعض اشیاء میں حکم کو بذریعہ عقل ادراک کیا جا سکتا ہے شرع پر موقوف نہیں ہے اور بعض حنفیہ بھی اسی بات کے قائل ہیں۔ البتہ دونوں مذہب میں اس طرح فرق کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ بعض احکام جن میں بعض حنفیہ عقل کو مستقل مانتے ہیں بہت قلیل ہے جیسے ایمان و کفر وغیرہ ۔ اور معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل سے مدرک احکام کثیر ہیں بعض اعاظم نے دونوں میں فرق کرتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک ہیں اور تح نفس الامر میں جمیع احکام کو ستلزم ہے گو کہ عقل صرف بعض ہی فعل کے حسن و قبیح کا ادراک کرتی ہے اور ماترید میہ نے اگر چہ یہ تصریح کر دی ہے کہ عقل بعض احکام کے ادراک کرنے میں مستقل ہے اور ان لوگوں نے نہیں کہا ہے کہ حسن و قبح سے جمیع احکام کا ادراک کر لیتی ہے تاہم بعض حنفیہ کے مذہب پر تحکم کا الزام عائد ہوگا کیونکہ عقل کو بعض افعال کے لئے مسلم حکم قرار دیا اور بعض کے لئے نہیں محکم محض ہے۔ حاصل نزاع: امام فخر الاسلام کے کلام سے مفہوم ہوتا ہے کہ ماتریدیہ، معتزلہ اور اشاعرہ کے مابین حاصل نزاع یہ ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل علت موجبہ ہے حکم کے لئے اور اشعریہ کے نزدیک علت محمدرہ اور ماتریدیہ کے نزدیک عقل نہ غلت موجبہ ہے نہ علت محمد رہ بلکہ عقل اہلبیت حکم کو اور علیم وخبیر سے تعلق محکم کو واجب کرتی ہے۔
قوله وروى عن أبى حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى الخ اس عبارت سے بعض حنفیہ کے موقف کی تائید و توثیق ہوتی ہے کہ احناف کے امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ کی روایت سے ظاہر ہے کہ عقل بعض احکام کے ادراک میں مستقل ہے چنانچہ کتاب منتقلی میں، پھر امیز ان میں محمد بین ساعت عن محمد بن الحسن اور جامع الاسرار وغیرہ میں عن ابی یوسف عن ابی حنیفہ مروی ہے کہ کوئی عاقل اپنے خالق سے جاہل رہ جانے کے سبب قیامت کے دن معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا بلکہ معذب ہوگا کیونکہ وہ خالق کے وجود، اس کی وحدانیت اور اس کے صفات کمالیہ کے ثبوت پر، آسمان وز مین کی پیدائش، اختلاف لیل و نہار و دیگر مخلوقات و عجائب قدرت اور خود اپنی خلقت جیسے دلائل و شواہد کا مشاہدہ کر رہا ہے اس لئے اسمیں قطعا شک کی گنجائش نہیں۔ عقلاً معرفت الہی کو ثابت کرتے ہوئے اس جگہ فاضل خیر آبادی نے کہا ہے۔ حق یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ اور اس کے صفات پر ایمان صفت کمال ہے اور اس سے کفر جمیع عقلاء کے نزدیک صفت نقصان ہے نیز ایمان شکر نعمت ہے اور شکر نعمت صفت کمال ہے اور کفر، کفران نعمت ہے اور کفران نعمت صفت نقصان ہے پس منقل کے نزدیک ایمان صفت حسن اور کفر فتیح ہو گا اس لئے لازم ہے کر عقل حسن کی طرف رغبت رکھے اور فتح سے بے رغبتی کا اظہار کرے لہذا جو بندہ امر مستحسن عقلی کو ترک کرے گا معاقب ہوگا معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا گر چہ اللہ عز شانہ کی طرف سے کوئی حکم نہیں آیا ہو۔
His statement: 'Then among the Hanafis is one who said that intellect...' meaning: Some Hanafi scholars, such as Imam Alam al-Huda Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Imam Fakhr al-Islam Sahib al-Mizan, and Sadr al-Shari'a, etc., said that intellect, without seeking aid from the Shari'a, can independently perceive some of the rulings of Allah the Exalted due to the goodness and evilness of actions. Accordingly, these noble scholars declared it obligatory upon every mature sane person, even a sane child, whether the call of the Messenger reached him or not, to believe in Allah the Exalted, and declared forbidden all things that are not befitting the majesty of Allah the Exalted, such as injustice and foolishness, etc., because intellect has indicated the goodness of faith and the evilness of disbelief. The summary is that according to these scholars, rulings are of two types: one in the perception of which intellect is not independent, so intellect cannot impose obligation or prohibition, etc. The second is that in which intellect is independent in perceiving it without the aid of Shari'a. So intellect can impose obligation and prohibition in it. In short, the madhhab of these some Hanafis lies between the madhhab of the majority Hanafis and the madhhab of the Mu'tazila: the majority Hanafis claim that intellect absolutely does not entail a ruling, and the Mu'tazila claim that intellect absolutely entails a ruling. Separate from both of these, the madhhab of some Hanafis is that intellect entails a ruling in some matters and does not entail a ruling in others. From our exposition it becomes clear that there is a clear difference between the madhhab of some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila. Therefore, the statement of some commentators of Musallam that there is no difference between some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila, and then their attempt to answer is a lengthy fruitless endeavor. Indeed, before me now are three reliable Arabic commentaries on Musallam in which it is said that there is no difference between some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila in this issue because the Mu'tazila hold that in some matters the ruling can be perceived by intellect, not dependent on Shari'a, and some Hanafis also hold the same. However, a difference can be made between the two madhhabs in this way: the rulings regarding which some Hanafis consider intellect independent are very few, like faith and disbelief. And according to the Mu'tazila, the rulings perceived by intellect are many. Some great scholars have said, distinguishing between them, that according to the Mu'tazila, intellect in itself entails all rulings, even though intellect only perceives the goodness and evilness of some actions. And the Maturidis, although they have stated that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings, they did not say that it perceives all rulings through goodness and evilness. Nevertheless, the madhhab of some Hanafis will face the accusation of arbitrariness because they have given intellect a decisive ruling for some actions and not for others, which is mere arbitration. The essence of the dispute: From the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam it is understood that the dispute among the Maturidis, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris is that the Mu'tazila consider intellect an efficient cause (illa mujiba) for the ruling, the Ash'aris consider it an unprepared cause (illa muhammira), and the Maturidis consider it neither an efficient cause nor an unprepared cause, rather intellect makes the ruling necessary and from the relationship of the One Who Knows and is Aware to the ruling it makes it necessary.
His statement: 'And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him...' This phrase supports and confirms the position of some Hanafis. From the narration of the great Imam of the Hanafis, Abu Hanifa, it is apparent that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings. Indeed, in the book Muntala, then in... [؟] Muhammad bin... [؟] from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, and in Jami' al-Asrar, etc., from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that no sane person will be considered excused on the Day of Resurrection due to being ignorant of his Creator, but rather will be punished, because he observes evidence and proofs for the existence of the Creator, His Oneness, and His attributes of perfection, such as the creation of the heavens and earth, the alternation of night and day, other creatures and wonders of power, and his own creation. Therefore, there is absolutely no room for doubt. Proving knowledge of God intellectually, at this point, the virtuous al-Khayrabadi said: The truth is that faith in Allah and His attributes is an attribute of perfection, and disbelief in Him is, according to all rational people, an attribute of deficiency. Also, faith is gratitude for blessings, and gratitude for blessings is an attribute of perfection, and disbelief is ingratitude for blessings, and ingratitude for blessings is an attribute of deficiency. So according to intellect, faith is an attribute of goodness and disbelief is an attribute of evilness. Therefore, it is necessary that intellect inclines towards goodness and shows aversion to evilness. Hence, a servant who abandons a rationally good action will be punished, not considered excused, even if no command has come from Allah, Exalted is His Glory.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage from 'Ashraf al-Nu'ut' is a commentary on 'Musallam al-Thubut', discussing the epistemological question of whether the human intellect ('aql) can independently arrive at Shari'ah rulings without revelation. The author explains that some Hanafi scholars (particularly the Maturidis: Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Sadr al-Shari'a) hold that intellect can independently perceive the moral value (husn/qubh) of some actions. They therefore rule that faith (iman) in God is obligatory upon every sane person, even a child, regardless of whether the prophetic call reached them, because intellect recognizes the goodness of faith and the evilness of disbelief. Similarly, actions like injustice are forbidden by intellect alone. These scholars distinguish between rulings that intellect can perceive (like the necessity of belief and prohibition of disbelief) and those it cannot (like details of prayer, fasting, etc.). The author then positions this view as a middle ground between the majority Hanafis (who deny any independent legal force for intellect) and the Mu'tazila (who claim intellect is the sole determinant of all rulings irrespective of revelation). He rebuts claims by some commentators of Musallam that there is no difference between these some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila, pointing out that the Hanafis limit intellect's scope to very few rulings, while the Mu'tazila extend it to many. He further distinguishes the Maturidi position from Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: Mu'tazila see intellect as an efficient cause, Ash'aris as a mere indicator, and Maturidis as making the ruling necessary through knowledge of God. The passage then provides a narration from Abu Hanifa (through chains in Muntala, Jami' al-Asrar) stating that ignorance of the Creator is no excuse because rational evidence (the universe, etc.) proves His existence and attributes. Finally, al-Khayrabadi's reasoning is quoted: faith is perfection and gratitude, disbelief is deficiency and ingratitude, so rational good must be pursued and evil avoided; hence punishment for abandoning rationally good acts even without revelation.

Key Terms
عقل ('aql): The human faculty of reason that can independently perceive the moral value (husn/qubh) of some actions and thereby derive certain Shari'ah rulings (e.g., obligation of iman, prohibition of kufr) without revelation.
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): The inherent goodness or evilness of acts as perceived by intellect; acts are not morally neutral but have intrinsic qualities that reason can discern.
علة موجبة (illa mujiba): An efficient cause that necessarily produces its effect; here, Mu'tazila view intellect as the efficient cause of rulings.
رواية (riwayah): A narration; here referring to the transmission from Abu Hanifa through multiple chains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in the narration from Abu Hanifa for why a sane person ignorant of the Creator is not excused?
A) The person has seen miracles.
B) The person observes evidence of God's existence in creation.
C) The person was born with innate knowledge.
D) The person is accountable for seeking knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The narration from Abu Hanifa provides textual support for the Maturidi position: rational evidence is sufficient to obligate belief in God.

Failure of Ash'ari Proof for Shar'i Husn/Qubh
Response to Ash'ari proof that husn/qubh is purely shar'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Ash'ari proof for shar'i husn/qubh is not conclusive against Hanafis and al-Jubba'i, and evaluate the distinction between two types of iqtida' dhati.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari proof fails because 'intrinsic' can mean either an inseparable property or one overridden by accidents—Hanafis deny exclusivity.

In this passage, the commentator critiques the Ash'ari argument that goodness and evil must be purely revealed because if they were intrinsic, naskh would be impossible. The author shows that this argument only works against a simplistic definition of 'intrinsic'. Al-Jubba'i does not accept intrinsic value at all, and Hanafis do not restrict goodness/evil to a single category. By distinguishing two senses of iqtida' dhati, the Hanafi response neutralizes the Ash'ari proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targeted opponents
Recognize that the Ash'ari proof aims to refute everyone who believes in intrinsic goodness/evil. The passage notes it fails against al-Jubba'i (who denies intrinsic) and Hanafis (who deny exclusivity).
Al-Jubba'i holds that husn/qubh are i'tibari (considerational), not intrinsic.
2
Understand the Ash'ari proof
The Ash'ari argument: if an act were intrinsically good, it could never become evil; but abrogation shows acts change from good to evil. Therefore goodness/evil must be from revelation, not intrinsic.
3
Analyze Hanafi objection to exclusivity
Hanafis do not limit husn/qubh to either intrinsic or revealed exclusively. They accept that rulings are from Shari'ah but do not deny rational recognition; thus the proof's dichotomy is false.
This is the 'denial of exclusivity' (h?sr) mentioned.
4
Apply the distinction of iqtida' dhati
The Hanafi response uses two meanings: (1) inseparable intrinsic (e.g., marriage implies possibility of four wives) and (2) overridable intrinsic (e.g., coldness of water overcome by heating). The Ash'ari proof assumes only the first meaning.
The cold water analogy illustrates that an intrinsic property can be temporarily overridden by an accident.
5
Evaluate the overall force of the proof
Since the Ash'ari proof only refutes an inflexible intrinsicism, it fails to be conclusive against positions that allow external factors or deny exclusivity. The commentator suggests even against majority Mu'tazila the proof is incomplete.
Thus the truth is more nuanced; the proof is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله - على انه لايتم على الجبائية وعلينا حسن و قبح کے شرعی ہونے پر اشاعرہ نے جو دلیل دی ہے اس کی دلیل کا یہ دوسرا جواب ہے اس عبارت سے مصنف کہتے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کی دلیل مذکور جبائیہ اور ہم حنفیہ کے خلاف تام نہیں ہوتی ہے کیونکہ جبائیہ فعل کا حسن و قبح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں مانتے بلکہ صفت اعتباریہ کا قول کرتے ہیں اور ہم حنفیہ اطلاق عام کا قول کرتے ہیں البتہ یہ دلیل جمہور معتزلہ کے خلاف ضرور قائم ہوسکتی ہے جو حسن و قبح کے ذاتی ہونے کے قائل ہیں چونکہ یہ دلیل ذاتی ہونے کو منع کر رہی ہے اور رہے جبائیہ تو وہ اس کے قائل نہیں اور حنفیہ تو وہ حصر سے انکار کرتے ہیں۔ حق یہ ہے کہ یہ دلیل جمہور معتزلہ پر بھی تام نہیں ہوتی چنانچہ نا سبق میں گذر چکا ہے کہ اقتضاء ذاتی کا اطلاق دو معنوں پر ہوتا ہے ایک وہ مقتضی جس کی ذات بلا شرط زائد کے مقتضی ہو جیسے زوجیت اربعہ کے لئے پس ایسی ذاتی ( مقتضی ) کا ذات سے تخلف بالکل ممتنع ہے دوسرا وہ مقتضی کہ ذات اس کو من حیث ھی بھی چاہے جیسے برودت ماء کہ اس ذاتی کا ذات سے تخلف بعروض عارض جائز ہے چنانچہ برودت ما تسخن عارض ہونے کے وقت زائل
His statement: 'But it is not complete against al-Jubba'i and against us.' This is the second answer to the proof that the Ash'aris gave for the revealed nature of goodness and evil. Through this expression, the author says that the mentioned proof of the Ash'aris is not complete against al-Jubba'i and us Hanafis. Because al-Jubba'i does not believe that an act's goodness and evil are due to the act's essence; rather, he holds that they are an accidental attribute. And we Hanafis hold that they are general applicability. However, this proof can certainly stand against the majority of Mu'tazila who believe in the intrinsic nature of goodness and evil, because this proof forbids intrinsicness. As for al-Jubba'i, he does not believe in it. And as for the Hanafis, they deny exclusivity. The truth is that this proof is not complete even against the majority of Mu'tazila, as has passed in the preceding discussion that the 'intrinsic entailment' (iqtida' dhati) applies in two senses: one is an entailing factor whose essence entails without additional condition, like the marital relationship (zawjiyya) for the number four—so such an intrinsic factor cannot possibly deviate from its essence. The second is an entailing factor that the essence also requires from its own perspective, like the coldness of water—deviation of this intrinsic factor from its essence is permissible by the occurrence of an accident. Thus, the coldness of water ceases when heating occurs...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Ash'ari argument that goodness and evil are purely revealed (shar'i) and not intrinsic. The commentator explains that the Ash'ari proof does not refute al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazilite who denies intrinsic moral value) because al-Jubba'i does not claim intrinsic goodness/evil. It also does not refute the Hanafis, because Hanafis do not claim that actions have intrinsic value; rather, they affirm general applicability of divine commands. The proof only targets the majority Mu'tazila who hold intrinsic goodness. However, even against them, the proof is not conclusive because 'intrinsic' has two meanings. The first meaning is an inseparable property (like the number four in marriage—marriage cannot be without the possibility of four wives in some contexts). The second meaning is a property that naturally arises from the essence but can be overridden by external factors (like coldness of water, which disappears when heated). So the Ash'ari argument that something intrinsically good can never become evil uses the first meaning, but the Mu'tazila may be using the second meaning, which allows change. Thus, the Ash'ari proof fails to demonstrate that all moral values must be revealed; it only shows that if goodness were intrinsic in the first sense, it could not change. The Hanafi position is that rulings are based on divine command, not intrinsic properties, but the passage engages in a dialectical argument to show the weakness of the opposition's reasoning.

Key Terms
اقتضاء ذاتی (iqtida' dhati): Intrinsic entailment; a property that arises from the essence of something. Two types: one that cannot be separated, another that can be overridden by external factors.
شرعی (shar'i): Revealed; pertaining to divine law. Ash'aris hold that goodness/evil are strictly shar'i.
صفة اعتباریہ (sifa i'tibariyya): Considerational attribute; a quality that is not real but mentally considered. Al-Jubba'i's view that goodness/evil are i'tibari.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the Ash'ari proof for shar'i husn/qubh not complete against the Hanafis, according to the passage?
A) Because Hanafis do not believe in any intrinsic goodness at all.
B) Because Hanafis deny the claim of exclusivity—they do not limit goodness/evil to only intrinsic or only revealed.
C) Because Hanafis hold that actions are evil by essence.
D) Because Hanafis agree with the Ash'ari argument completely.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof against intrinsic goodness fails because it targets only one interpretation of 'intrinsic'; Hanafis reject the exclusivity that would force a choice between intrinsic and revealed.

First Reason for Particularization: Avoiding Futility in Divine Creation
First rational proof for takhsis of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi argument that general verses of creation must be particularized to exclude intention to avoid absurdity in Allah's creation of power.

Core Idea
If intention is not excluded, Allah's creation of power in man becomes futile.

The Hanafi school defends a middle path between jabr and tafwid. The first rational argument is based on the necessity of avoiding futility in divine action. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it leads to the particularization of intention from general creation texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Recognize that Allah creates power (qudra) in the servant before any action. This power must have some benefit; otherwise creation would be futile.
Quran 21:16 establishes that Allah's actions are not futile.
2
Analyze the entailment
If the created power has no effect, then creating it is futile (ʿabath). Since Allah is free from futility, the power must be effective in at least one thing.
3
Apply the principle of minimal benefit
The highest effect would be full efficacy in all actions, but that leads to tafwid (Mu'tazilite view). The lowest effect is influence over intention (qasd musammam). Therefore, intention must be created by the servant to give power some benefit.
4
Conclude the particularization
General verses like 'Allah created you and what you do' are thus particularized: they apply to all actions except intention, which is explicitly excluded by rational necessity.

جواب :۔ یہ جواب جمہور حنفیہ کی طرف سے ہے۔ جواب کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ قصد مصمم حال نہیں ہے جیسا کہ کہ مجیب اول کا خیال ہے کیونکہ حال بذاتہ محال ہوتا ہے وجہ استعمالہ یہ ہے کہ وجودشی اور عدم شمی کے درمیان واسطہ غیر معقول ہے۔ صحیح یہ ہے کہ وہ قصد خارج میں موجود ہے جس میں بندہ کے اندر پیدا کرد و قدرت اثر انداز ہوتی ہے یا جسکو بندہ اپنی قدرت و اختیار سے کسب کرتا ہے رہے وہ نصوص جن میں خلق کی استاد بندو کی طرف ممتنع ہے تو ان نصوص کے حکم عام سے قصد کو دو اہم ضرورات شرعیہ کی وجہ سے خاص کر لیا گیا ہے چنانچہ آیت والله خلقکم و ماتعملون وغيرها آیات مبارکہ جن میں علی الاطلاق و بالعموم خلق کی استاد جناب باری تعالی کی طرف ہے جن کا مفاد یہ ہیکہ عباد کے سارے افعال کا خالق اللہ عز شانہ تعالٰی ہے مگر ان افعال سے صرف فعل قصد بر بنائے ضرورت مستقلی ہے۔ بصورت تخصیص قصد آیات کے معنی یہ ہونگے کہ سوائے قصد کے جملہ افعال کا خالق اللہ تعالیٰ ہے اور رہا قصد تو اس کا موجد بندہ ہے۔
تخصیص قصد کی وجہ اول : خلق سے قصد کو خاص کرنے کی پہلی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اگر خلق سے قصد کو خاص نہ کیا جائے گا تو فصل باری تعالیٰ کا عبث والغو ہو تالا زم آئے گا والملازم باطل فالملزوم دوم مثلہ بیشک اللہ عز شانہ کا فعل عیث والفو سے پاک و منزہ ہے ارشاد ہے وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما لعبين جب اللہ تعالیٰ کے جعل کا عبث سے منزہ ہو نا لا زم و ثابت ہے تو ماننا پڑے گا کہ خلق سے قصد مخصوص ہے ورنہ عبث ضرور لازم آئے گا۔
ویہ ملازمت یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ بندہ کے قصد وارادہ اور ایجاد فعل سے پہلے بندہ میں فعل کی قدرت کا خلق فرماتا ہے جس کا مفاد و مقتضی یہ ہے کہ بندہ اس قدرت مخلوقہ کے ذریعہ شٹکی میں تاثیر دے کیونکہ فعل الہی گرچہ معلل بالاغراض ہونے کی وجہ سے پاک ہے تا ہم اس کا کوئی فعل حکمت ومصلحت اور غایات مودعہ سے خالی و عاری نہیں ہے اور نہ لغو ہو جائے گا۔ پس اللہ نے بندہ میں جس قدرت کا خلق فرمایا ہے اس قدرت کا کوئی فائدہ ہے یا نہیں اگر اس قدرت مخلوقہ کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے بلکہ بے فائدہ ہے تو خلق قدرت عبث و لغو شہر او حو منزه عن ذالک، اور بندہ بھی مجبور ہوا اور یہ دونوں ظاہر البطلان ہیں۔ اور اگر وہ قدرت بے فائدہ نہیں بلکہ فائدہ مند پیدا کی گئی ہے تو قدرت مفید و کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ بندہ کونفل و ترک اور قصد وارادہ پر مکمل قدرت و اختیار حاصل رہے اگر اتنا اختیار نہ ہو تو کم از کم قصد مصمم کی طرف قدرت کو پھیرنے کا ضرور اختیار حاصل ہوگا تا کہ خلق قدرت کا ادنی فائد متحقق ہو سکے ، اس لئے کہ خلق قدرت کا اعلیٰ درجہ تو یہ ہے کہ بندہ اس قدرت سے شی میں مؤثر و دخیل ہو اور ادنیٰ درجہ یہ ہے کہ شئی میں نہیں تو کم از کم قصد شنی میں اثر انداز ہو۔ لہذا ہم نے اعلیٰ کو ترک کیا تا کہ تفویض لازم نہ آئے اور ادنیٰ کو ترک نہیں کیا تا کہ جبر نہ لازم آئے۔ ماتن کا قول لانہ ادنی ما تحقق به فائدة خلق القدرة “ اسی مفہوم مذکور کی طرف مشیر ہے ۔
قوله - ويتجه به حسن التكليف
وجه دوم : خلق سے قصد کو خاص کرنے کی دوسری وجہ بیان کی ہے کہ اگر نصوص خلق سے قصد کو خاص نہیں کیا گیا تو تکلیف عباد ممتنع ہو جائے گی اور اللہ عز شانہ کی طرف ظلم وجور کی نسبت جائز ٹھہریگی واللازم باطل فالملزوم مثلہ وجہ ملازمت یہ ہے کہ قصد مصمم کو اگر مخلوق عبد نہ فرض کیا جائے تو بندہ اس صورت میں قدرت ، قصد ، ارادہ وغیرہ صدور افعال کے جملہ لوازم و عوارض سے خالی اور عاری ہوگا بلکہ غیر قادر اور عاجز رہے گا اور عاجز غیر قادر کو اعمال و افعال کا مکلف کرنا عقل ممتنع ہے پھر اعمال حسنہ اور افعال سید کے ترک دار تکاب پر عقاب و عذاب کا استحقاق ٹھہرا نا صریح ظلم ہے کہ بندہ پہلے ہی سے عاجز تھا۔ لیکن اگر قصد کو مخلوق عبد فرض کر لیا جائے تو اس کا ادنی فائدہ یہ ہوگا کہ بندے کو افعال و اعمال کا مکلف بنانا جائز وصحیح ہوگا اور باری تعالیٰ کی طرف ظلم و جور کا استناد کرنا بھی نہ لازم آئے گا کہ من وجہ بندہ میں قدرت پائی گئی بھر محض نہ رہا دھوکاف للتکلیف فیستحق العقاب بنتر کھا۔
قوله وهذا كانه واسطة بين الجبر والتفويض
اس عبارت سے بتانا چاہتے ہیں کہ قصد مصم کو مخلوق عبد ماننے سے واضح ہوتا ہے کہ مذہب حنفیہ، مذہب تفویض اور مذہب جبر کے درمیان واسطہ ہے۔ تفویض اس لئے نہیں ہے کہ جملہ افعال کو من کل الوجوہ بندوں کی طرف نہیں سوچنے جیسا کہ معتزلہ کہتے ہیں کہ بندہ اپنے جمیع افعال کا خالق ہے۔ اور جبر اس لئے نہیں کہ قدرت عبد کی تاثیر قصد مصمم میں بندہ کے لئے ثابت مانتے ہیں جبریہ اور اشاعرہ کی طرح بندہ کو مجبور نہیں گردانے ۔
Answer: This is the answer from the majority of the Hanafis. The summary of the answer is that the determinate intention is not a state (haal) as the first respondent thought, because a state in itself is impossible. The reason for its usage is that an intermediary between existence and non-existence of a thing is inconceivable. The correct view is that this intention exists externally, in which the power created within the servant is effective, or which the servant acquires with his power and choice. As for those texts in which the attribution of creation to the servant is prohibited, then the general ruling of these texts has been particularized to exclude intention due to two important Shari'i necessities. Thus, the verse 'Allah created you and what you do' and other blessed verses attribute creation absolutely and generally to Allah Almighty, their implication being that Allah is the Creator of all actions of servants. But from those actions, only the act of intention is excluded based on a separate necessity. With the particularization of intention, the meaning of the verses becomes: Allah is the Creator of all actions except intention; as for intention, its originator is the servant.
First reason for particularization of intention: The first reason for making intention specific from creation is that if intention is not made specific from creation, then the action of Allah Almighty would entail futility and frivolity, and that which necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. The concomitant (that Allah's action is free from futility) is established by His saying, 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever between them in jest.' Since it is established and certain that Allah's action is free from futility, it must be accepted that intention is particularized from creation; otherwise, futility would necessarily be entailed.
And this entailment is that Allah Almighty creates in the servant the power to act before the servant's intention, will, and bringing the act into existence. The benefit and requirement of this is that the servant should, through this created power, effect influence in the action, because although Allah's act is pure (being based on purposes), none of His actions are devoid of wisdom, benefit, and inherent purposes, nor will they be futile. So the power that Allah created in the servant has some benefit or not. If this created power has no benefit but is useless, then creating power would be futile and frivolous—and He is free from that—and the servant would also be coerced; both are evidently false. And if that power is not useless but is created beneficial, then the requirement of beneficial power is that the servant have complete power and choice over action, abandonment, intention, and will. If he does not have that much authority, then at least he must have the authority to direct the power towards the determinate intention, so that the minimal benefit of creating power may be realized. For the highest degree of creating power is that the servant, through this power, be effective and operative in the action, and the lowest degree is that if not in the action itself, then at least it has an effect in the intention of the action. Therefore, we abandoned the highest to avoid necessitating tafwid (delegation), and we did not abandon the lowest to avoid necessitating jabr (compulsion). The author's statement 'because it is the least by which the benefit of creating power is realized' points to this meaning.
His statement: 'And through it, the goodness of obligation is achieved.'
Second reason: The second reason for particularizing intention from creation is explained: if intention is not particularized from the texts of creation, then the obligation of servants would become impossible, and attributing injustice and oppression to Allah Almighty would become permissible—and the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. The reason for the entailment is that if determinate intention is not assumed to be created by the servant, then in that case the servant would be devoid and empty of power, intention, will, and all other requisites and concomitants of performing actions; rather, he would remain incapable and powerless. And to obligate actions and deeds upon the incapable and powerless is rationally impossible. Then, establishing entitlement to punishment and torment on account of abandoning good deeds and virtuous actions is clear injustice, because the servant was already incapable. But if intention is assumed to be created by the servant, then its minimal benefit is that making the servant obligated for actions and deeds becomes valid and correct, and the attribution of injustice and oppression to Allah Almighty will not be necessitated, because from one aspect the servant is found to have power, so he is not purely a subject of compulsion, and thus he is worthy of obligation and he deserves punishment for abandoning it.
His statement: 'And this is, as it were, an intermediary between jabr and tafwid.'
With this phrase, they intend to show that by accepting the determinate intention as created by the servant, it becomes clear that the Hanafi school is an intermediary between the school of tafwid and the school of jabr. It is not tafwid because they do not attribute all actions absolutely to the servants as the Mu'tazila say that the servant is the creator of all his actions. And it is not jabr because they affirm for the servant the effectiveness of his power in the determinate intention; they do not regard the servant as compelled like the Jabriyya and Ash'ara.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi theological defense of the doctrine of human intention (qasd musammam) and its creation by the servant, against an objection. The author, commenting on al-Ṣubkī's 'Musallam al-Thubūt', presents the Hanafi position as a middle path between Qadariyya/Mu'tazila (who delegate all creation to man) and Jabriyya/Ash'ariyya (who deny human efficacy). The core argument is that the general Qur'anic verses attributing creation of all actions to Allah (e.g., 'Allah created you and what you do') are particularized (takhṣīṣ) in the case of human intention, based on two Shari'i necessities: (1) Avoiding absurdity (ʿabath) in Allah's creation of power in humans—if the created power has no effect, creation is futile; the minimal effect is to influence intention. (2) Making taklīf (moral obligation) possible—if humans have no control over intention, they are incapable, and obligating them would be unjust. Thus, the servant creates his own intention, while Allah creates the action itself. This is not tafwīḍ (full delegation) because only intention is created by man; nor jabr (compulsion) because man has real efficacy in intention. The commentator follows the matn's structure, first stating the Hanafi answer, then the two reasons with rational entailments, and finally the intermediate position.

Key Terms
قصد مصمم (qasd musammam): Determinate intention; the deliberate resolve to perform an action, which the text argues is created by the servant.
خلق (khalq): Creation; bringing something into existence from nothing. Used in the context of divine creation of actions and human acts.
تكليف (taklīf): Moral obligation; the imposition of religious duties upon the servant, requiring capacity (qudra) and will (irāda).
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the doctrine that humans have no real efficacy and are forced by God.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation; the doctrine that humans create their own actions (Mu'tazila).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first Hanafi argument, what is the minimal benefit of creating power in the servant?
A) It enables intention.
B) It enables all actions.
C) It prevents jabr.
D) It proves divine omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
The first Hanafi reason for particularizing intention is that Allah's creation of power in man must have a benefit; the least benefit is that the servant's power influences his intention.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Second Reason for Particularization: Enabling Moral Obligation (Taklif)
Second rational proof for takhsis of intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the general creation texts must exclude intention to make moral obligation (taklif) meaningful and avoid attributing injustice to Allah.

Core Idea
Without intention created by the servant, obligating the incapable would be unjust and rationally impossible.

The second Hanafi argument for particularizing intention from divine creation is the necessity of enabling taklif. If intention were also created by Allah, the servant would have no control over his actions, making reward and punishment unjust. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Recognize that taklif requires the servant to have effective power over the act he is obligated to perform. Without power over intention, he has no control over the initiating motive.
Quran 2:286: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.'
2
Analyze the entailment
If intention is created by Allah, the servant is incapable of forming his own intention, making him powerless. Obligating the powerless is rationally impossible (mustahil aqli) and inherently unjust.
3
Apply the principle of divine justice
Attributing injustice to Allah is impossible. Therefore, the servant must have some creative power over intention to make obligation valid and just.
4
Conclude the particularization
Thus, general verses of creation must be particularized to exclude intention, so that taklif is meaningful and Allah's justice is preserved.

ویہ ملازمت یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ بندہ کے قصد وارادہ اور ایجاد فعل سے پہلے بندہ میں فعل کی قدرت کا خلق فرماتا ہے جس کا مفاد و مقتضی یہ ہے کہ بندہ اس قدرت مخلوقہ کے ذریعہ شٹکی میں تاثیر دے کیونکہ فعل الہی گرچہ معلل بالاغراض ہونے کی وجہ سے پاک ہے تا ہم اس کا کوئی فعل حکمت ومصلحت اور غایات مودعہ سے خالی و عاری نہیں ہے اور نہ لغو ہو جائے گا۔ پس اللہ نے بندہ میں جس قدرت کا خلق فرمایا ہے اس قدرت کا کوئی فائدہ ہے یا نہیں اگر اس قدرت مخلوقہ کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے بلکہ بے فائدہ ہے تو خلق قدرت عبث و لغو شہر او حو منزه عن ذالک، اور بندہ بھی مجبور ہوا اور یہ دونوں ظاہر البطلان ہیں۔ اور اگر وہ قدرت بے فائدہ نہیں بلکہ فائدہ مند پیدا کی گئی ہے تو قدرت مفید و کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ بندہ کونفل و ترک اور قصد وارادہ پر مکمل قدرت و اختیار حاصل رہے اگر اتنا اختیار نہ ہو تو کم از کم قصد مصمم کی طرف قدرت کو پھیرنے کا ضرور اختیار حاصل ہوگا تا کہ خلق قدرت کا ادنی فائد متحقق ہو سکے ، اس لئے کہ خلق قدرت کا اعلیٰ درجہ تو یہ ہے کہ بندہ اس قدرت سے شی میں مؤثر و دخیل ہو اور ادنیٰ درجہ یہ ہے کہ شئی میں نہیں تو کم از کم قصد شنی میں اثر انداز ہو۔ لہذا ہم نے اعلیٰ کو ترک کیا تا کہ تفویض لازم نہ آئے اور ادنیٰ کو ترک نہیں کیا تا کہ جبر نہ لازم آئے۔ ماتن کا قول لانہ ادنی ما تحقق به فائدة خلق القدرة “ اسی مفہوم مذکور کی طرف مشیر ہے ۔
قوله - ويتجه به حسن التكليف
وجه دوم : خلق سے قصد کو خاص کرنے کی دوسری وجہ بیان کی ہے کہ اگر نصوص خلق سے قصد کو خاص نہیں کیا گیا تو تکلیف عباد ممتنع ہو جائے گی اور اللہ عز شانہ کی طرف ظلم وجور کی نسبت جائز ٹھہریگی واللازم باطل فالملزوم مثلہ وجہ ملازمت یہ ہے کہ قصد مصمم کو اگر مخلوق عبد نہ فرض کیا جائے تو بندہ اس صورت میں قدرت ، قصد ، ارادہ وغیرہ صدور افعال کے جملہ لوازم و عوارض سے خالی اور عاری ہوگا بلکہ غیر قادر اور عاجز رہے گا اور عاجز غیر قادر کو اعمال و افعال کا مکلف کرنا عقل ممتنع ہے پھر اعمال حسنہ اور افعال سید کے ترک دار تکاب پر عقاب و عذاب کا استحقاق ٹھہرا نا صریح ظلم ہے کہ بندہ پہلے ہی سے عاجز تھا۔ لیکن اگر قصد کو مخلوق عبد فرض کر لیا جائے تو اس کا ادنی فائدہ یہ ہوگا کہ بندے کو افعال و اعمال کا مکلف بنانا جائز وصحیح ہوگا اور باری تعالیٰ کی طرف ظلم و جور کا استناد کرنا بھی نہ لازم آئے گا کہ من وجہ بندہ میں قدرت پائی گئی بھر محض نہ رہا دھوکاف للتکلیف فیستحق العقاب بنتر کھا۔
قوله وهذا كانه واسطة بين الجبر والتفويض
اس عبارت سے بتانا چاہتے ہیں کہ قصد مصم کو مخلوق عبد ماننے سے واضح ہوتا ہے کہ مذہب حنفیہ، مذہب تفویض اور مذہب جبر کے درمیان واسطہ ہے۔ تفویض اس لئے نہیں ہے کہ جملہ افعال کو من کل الوجوہ بندوں کی طرف نہیں سوچنے جیسا کہ معتزلہ کہتے ہیں کہ بندہ اپنے جمیع افعال کا خالق ہے۔ اور جبر اس لئے نہیں کہ قدرت عبد کی تاثیر قصد مصمم میں بندہ کے لئے ثابت مانتے ہیں جبریہ اور اشاعرہ کی طرح بندہ کو مجبور نہیں گردانے ۔
Answer: This is the answer from the majority of the Hanafis. The summary of the answer is that the determinate intention is not a state (haal) as the first respondent thought, because a state in itself is impossible. The reason for its usage is that an intermediary between existence and non-existence of a thing is inconceivable. The correct view is that this intention exists externally, in which the power created within the servant is effective, or which the servant acquires with his power and choice. As for those texts in which the attribution of creation to the servant is prohibited, then the general ruling of these texts has been particularized to exclude intention due to two important Shari'i necessities. Thus, the verse 'Allah created you and what you do' and other blessed verses attribute creation absolutely and generally to Allah Almighty, their implication being that Allah is the Creator of all actions of servants. But from those actions, only the act of intention is excluded based on a separate necessity. With the particularization of intention, the meaning of the verses becomes: Allah is the Creator of all actions except intention; as for intention, its originator is the servant.
First reason for particularization of intention: The first reason for making intention specific from creation is that if intention is not made specific from creation, then the action of Allah Almighty would entail futility and frivolity, and that which necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. The concomitant (that Allah's action is free from futility) is established by His saying, 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever between them in jest.' Since it is established and certain that Allah's action is free from futility, it must be accepted that intention is particularized from creation; otherwise, futility would necessarily be entailed.
And this entailment is that Allah Almighty creates in the servant the power to act before the servant's intention, will, and bringing the act into existence. The benefit and requirement of this is that the servant should, through this created power, effect influence in the action, because although Allah's act is pure (being based on purposes), none of His actions are devoid of wisdom, benefit, and inherent purposes, nor will they be futile. So the power that Allah created in the servant has some benefit or not. If this created power has no benefit but is useless, then creating power would be futile and frivolous—and He is free from that—and the servant would also be coerced; both are evidently false. And if that power is not useless but is created beneficial, then the requirement of beneficial power is that the servant have complete power and choice over action, abandonment, intention, and will. If he does not have that much authority, then at least he must have the authority to direct the power towards the determinate intention, so that the minimal benefit of creating power may be realized. For the highest degree of creating power is that the servant, through this power, be effective and operative in the action, and the lowest degree is that if not in the action itself, then at least it has an effect in the intention of the action. Therefore, we abandoned the highest to avoid necessitating tafwid (delegation), and we did not abandon the lowest to avoid necessitating jabr (compulsion). The author's statement 'because it is the least by which the benefit of creating power is realized' points to this meaning.
His statement: 'And through it, the goodness of obligation is achieved.'
Second reason: The second reason for particularizing intention from creation is explained: if intention is not particularized from the texts of creation, then the obligation of servants would become impossible, and attributing injustice and oppression to Allah Almighty would become permissible—and the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. The reason for the entailment is that if determinate intention is not assumed to be created by the servant, then in that case the servant would be devoid and empty of power, intention, will, and all other requisites and concomitants of performing actions; rather, he would remain incapable and powerless. And to obligate actions and deeds upon the incapable and powerless is rationally impossible. Then, establishing entitlement to punishment and torment on account of abandoning good deeds and virtuous actions is clear injustice, because the servant was already incapable. But if intention is assumed to be created by the servant, then its minimal benefit is that making the servant obligated for actions and deeds becomes valid and correct, and the attribution of injustice and oppression to Allah Almighty will not be necessitated, because from one aspect the servant is found to have power, so he is not purely a subject of compulsion, and thus he is worthy of obligation and he deserves punishment for abandoning it.
His statement: 'And this is, as it were, an intermediary between jabr and tafwid.'
With this phrase, they intend to show that by accepting the determinate intention as created by the servant, it becomes clear that the Hanafi school is an intermediary between the school of tafwid and the school of jabr. It is not tafwid because they do not attribute all actions absolutely to the servants as the Mu'tazila say that the servant is the creator of all his actions. And it is not jabr because they affirm for the servant the effectiveness of his power in the determinate intention; they do not regard the servant as compelled like the Jabriyya and Ash'ara.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi theological defense of the doctrine of human intention (qasd musammam) and its creation by the servant, against an objection. The author, commenting on al-Ṣubkī's 'Musallam al-Thubūt', presents the Hanafi position as a middle path between Qadariyya/Mu'tazila (who delegate all creation to man) and Jabriyya/Ash'ariyya (who deny human efficacy). The core argument is that the general Qur'anic verses attributing creation of all actions to Allah (e.g., 'Allah created you and what you do') are particularized (takhṣīṣ) in the case of human intention, based on two Shari'i necessities: (1) Avoiding absurdity (ʿabath) in Allah's creation of power in humans—if the created power has no effect, creation is futile; the minimal effect is to influence intention. (2) Making taklīf (moral obligation) possible—if humans have no control over intention, they are incapable, and obligating them would be unjust. Thus, the servant creates his own intention, while Allah creates the action itself. This is not tafwīḍ (full delegation) because only intention is created by man; nor jabr (compulsion) because man has real efficacy in intention. The commentator follows the matn's structure, first stating the Hanafi answer, then the two reasons with rational entailments, and finally the intermediate position.

Key Terms
قصد مصمم (qasd musammam): Determinate intention; the deliberate resolve to perform an action, which the text argues is created by the servant.
خلق (khalq): Creation; bringing something into existence from nothing. Used in the context of divine creation of actions and human acts.
تكليف (taklīf): Moral obligation; the imposition of religious duties upon the servant, requiring capacity (qudra) and will (irāda).
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the doctrine that humans have no real efficacy and are forced by God.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation; the doctrine that humans create their own actions (Mu'tazila).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second Hanafi argument, what makes taklif impossible if intention is not particularized?
A) The servant would have no power over his intention, making him incapable.
B) The servant would be forced, which is logically impossible.
C) The servant would be created by Allah, which contradicts free will.
D) The servant would deserve punishment for no fault of his own.

Key Takeaway
The second Hanafi reason: Intention must be created by the servant so that taklif is just and meaningful, avoiding injustice to Allah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Lexical Generality of 'Rasul' and the Hanafi Refutation of Mu'tazilite Ta'wil
Textual analysis refuting Mu'tazilite ta'wil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of lexical generality (umum al-lughawi) to the term 'rasul' in Q. 17:15 and evaluate Mu'tazilite ta'wil against Hanafi usul criteria.

Core Idea
Reason is an internal messenger; the verse's condition covers all commands, not just those beyond rational reach.

This passage delves into a key usul dispute: does the Quranic verse 'mā kunnā muʿadhdhibīn hattā nabʿatha rasūlā' (We would not punish until We send a messenger) allow for rational obligation before revelation? The Hanafi school argues that 'messenger' includes both external prophets and internal reason, while the Mu'tazilites offered forced ta'wil to restrict the verse. You will learn to dissect the Hanafi position and critique the opposing ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated term
Locate the key term 'rasul' in the verse and note its lexical range. In Hanafi usul, the default is that a word retains its full lexical meaning unless there is evidence for restriction (takhsis).
Lexical meaning: 'one sent to convey a message'.
2
Apply the principle of 'amm al-lughawi'
The Hanafi view holds that 'rasul' is 'amm (general) in its lexical sense, covering both external and internal sendings. Therefore, reason is included as an internal messenger.
This is based on the usul maxim: al-amm yufidu al-shumul (the general indicates comprehensiveness).
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite ta'wil
The Mu'tazilites proposed two ta'wil: (1) 'rasul' is a particular term used for the universal 'warner'; (2) the verse means punishment is only for laws unreachable without revelation. Identify how these ta'wil depart from the apparent meaning (zahir).
In Hanafi usul, ta'wil is valid only if supported by strong dalil; here the author deems the ta'wil forced.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi refutation hinges on taking the verse at face value: the condition is general. The Mu'tazilite rational arguments are insufficient to override the lexical generality, so their ta'wil is rejected.
The author also notes that even if Mu'tazilite premises are accepted, punishment may still require revelation as a condition.
5
Derive the usul outcome
Conclude that the Hanafi interpretation is more literally and methodologically sound, maintaining that reason can perceive good and evil but punishment may still depend on revelation due to the verse's condition.
This establishes the Hanafi position on rational obligation (wujub 'aqli) vis-à-vis revelation.

Source Text
